Preserving Truth and Quality in Conversion
Author clarifies preserving truth and quality in conversion

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between preservation of truth (entailment) and preservation of quality in conversion, and correctly apply the condition of truth preservation to Islamic propositions.

Core Idea
Preservation of truth is about entailment, not actual truth.

When logicians say the converse must preserve truth, they don't mean both statements are true in reality. They mean that if the original is true, the converse must necessarily follow. Similarly, quality (affirmative/negative) must match. This lesson unpacks these two key conditions using the classic example 'Every animal is a human' to show how a false original can have a true converse.

Guided Path
1
Understand the definition of conversion
Conversion means swapping the first and second parts of a proposition. For categoricals, that means subject and predicate swap. The author deliberately uses 'first part' and 'second part' to also cover conditionals.
2
Grasp 'preserving truth' as entailment
Preserving truth does not require both original and converse to be actually true. It means: if the original were true, the converse would be true. This is a conditional entailment (luzum).
Example: 'Every animal is a human' is false, but its converse 'Some human is an animal' is true. The false original does not break the condition because the condition only applies when the original is assumed true.
3
Note that falsity is not preserved
The author explicitly states they did not require preservation of falsity, because a false original can have a true converse (as shown above). So a valid conversion does not need the converse to be false when the original is false.
4
Understand 'preserving quality'
Quality means affirmative or negative. The converse must have the same quality as the original. This was observed empirically: most conversions that are true and necessary only occur with the same quality.
Example: If original is 'No human is stone' (negative), the converse 'No stone is human' is also negative.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإنما قال : جعل الجزء الأول من القضية ثانياً والثاني أولاً»، لا تبديل الموضوع بالمحمول - كما ذكر بعضهم - ليشمل عكس الحمليات و الشرطيات. وليس المراد ببقاء الصدق أن العكس والأصل يكونان صادقين في الواقع، بل المراد أن الأصل يكون بحيث لو فرض صدقه لزم صدق العكس. وانما اعتبروا اللزوم في الصدق لأن العكس لازم من لوازم القضية، ويستحيل صدق الملزوم بدون صدق اللازم. ولم يعتبر بقاء الكذب إذ لم يلزم من كذب الملزوم كذب اللازم، فإنّ قولنا : «كل حيوان إنسان كاذب، مع صدق عكسه وهو قولنا «بعض الإنسان حيوان. والمراد ببقاء الكيف أن الأصل لو كان موجباً كان العكس أيضاً موجباً، وإن كان سالباً فسالباً، وانما وقع الاصطلاح عليه لأنهم تتبعوا القضايا فلم يجدوها في الأكثر بعد التبديل صادقة لازمة إلا موافقة لها في الكيف.
And he only said 'making the first part of the proposition second and the second first'—and not 'exchanging subject with predicate' as some have mentioned—in order to include the conversion of categoricals and conditionals. And what is meant by 'preserving truth' is not that the converse and the original are both true in reality, but rather that the original is such that if its truth is assumed, the truth of the converse follows necessarily. They only considered the entailment in truth because the converse is a necessary consequence of the proposition, and it is impossible for the entailing proposition to be true without the entailed being true. They did not consider preservation of falsity because the falsity of the entailing does not necessitate the falsity of the entailed. For our statement 'Every animal is a human' is false, while its converse 'Some human is an animal' is true. And what is meant by 'preserving quality' is that if the original is affirmative, the converse is also affirmative, and if it is negative, it is negative. The convention on this was adopted because they examined propositions and found, for the most part, that after the swap they are not true and necessary unless they agree in quality.
Source pages p. 344

Commentary
This passage expands on the definition of conversion by clarifying three points. First, the wording 'making the first part second and the second first' is deliberately chosen over 'exchanging subject with predicate' to cover both categorical and conditional propositions. Second, 'preserving truth' does not mean both original and converse are actually true; rather, it means a conditional entailment: if the original is true, then the converse must be true. The author illustrates with the proposition 'Every animal is a human' (false), whose converse 'Some human is an animal' is true. Preservation of falsity is not required because a false original can have a true converse. Third, 'preserving quality' means that the quality (affirmative/negative) is the same in both. This is an empirical observation: when logicians examined propositions, they found that most conversions that are necessarily true only occur when quality is preserved. This lesson is fundamental for understanding the mechanics of immediate inference in classical Islamic logic.

Key Terms
بقاء الصدق (baqa' al-sidq) - preservation of truth: the original entails the truth of the converse, not actual truth.
بقاء الكيف (baqa' al-kayf) - preservation of quality: the converse has the same affirmative/negative quality as the original.
لزوم (luzum) - necessary entailment: the converse is a necessary consequence of the original.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following correctly describes preservation of truth in conversion?
A) The original and converse must both be true in reality.
B) The original must be true, and the converse must be true.
C) If the original were true, the converse would be true (entailment).
D) The converse must be true regardless of the original.

Key Takeaway
Preservation of truth means entailment: if the original is true, the converse must be true; and preservation of quality means the converse must match the original in being affirmative or negative.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Task 1: For the proposition 'Every intoxicant is forbidden' (true in Islamic law), convert it. Check if the condition of preservation of truth holds: if the original is true, does the converse also follow as true? (Assume the original is true.)
?
Task 2: Explain why the proposition 'No animal is a human' (false) and its converse 'No human is an animal' (false) still satisfy preservation of truth despite both being false. (Hint: the condition only cares about entailment when the original is assumed true.)

Seven Universal Negatives That Do Not Convert
Author lists seven universal negatives that do not convert and proves by the most specific case

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the seven modal types of universal negative propositions that do not convert and explain why using the temporal example and the conditional proof based on 'lāzim al-lāzim lāzim'.

Core Idea
Universal negatives of seven modal types fail conversion; the proof hinges on the most specific case and a logical chain.

This passage from Qutbi's commentary addresses the conversion of universal negative propositions. While some universal negatives do convert, the author lists seven modal types that do not. To prove this, he uses a concrete example involving the moon and eclipses and a conditional argument that relies on the principle that the necessary concomitant of a necessary concomitant is itself necessary. You will learn to distinguish these non-converting negatives and understand the underlying reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the seven types
Locate in the Arabic text the list: two temporal (waqtiyya), two existential (wujūdiyya), two possible (mumkina), and one general absolute (muṭlaqa ʿāmma). These are the universal negatives that do not convert.
The temporal is the most specific; the others are more general.
2
Understand the temporal example
The author gives an example: 'Necessarily, nothing of the moon is eclipsed at the time of quadrature, not always.' This is a true temporal universal negative. Its purported converse is 'Some eclipse is not a moon with general possibility,' which is false because every eclipse is necessarily a moon. This shows conversion fails for the temporal.
The converse uses the 'general possibility' modality, the most general.
3
Follow the conditional proof
The author argues: if the general types (e.g., existential, possible) did convert, then the specific temporal would also convert. This is because the converse (the result of conversion) is a necessary concomitant of the general, and the general is a necessary concomitant of the specific. Since the temporal does not convert, the general cannot convert either.
This uses the principle 'lāzim al-lāzim lāzim' (the necessary concomitant of a necessary concomitant is necessary).
4
Apply to Islamic sciences
Consider a fiqh example: the universal negative 'No transaction involving riba is valid.' Its converse 'No valid transaction involves riba' is true? Actually, the correct conversion would require the converse to be a universal negative, but here the converse is true? We need a non-converting example. Use the temporal example directly: In Islamic astronomy (falak), the proposition about the moon was studied. Alternatively, in usul al-fiqh, a universal negative like 'No prayer is valid without purity' converts, but if it were a temporal negative ('No prayer is valid during menstruation necessarily, not always'), it would not convert. This distinction is critical for jurists when applying 'aks.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال: أما السوالب فإن كانت كلية فسبع منها - وهي الوقتيتان، والوجوديتان، والممكنتان، والمطلقة العامة - لا تنعكس، لامتناع العكس في أخصها - وهي الوقتية - لصدق قولنا: بالضرورة لاشيء من القمر بمنخسف وقت التربيع لادائماً وكذب قولك: «بعض المنخسف ليس بقمر بالإمكان العام الذي هو أعمّ الجهات، لأنّ كلّ منخسف فهو قمر بالضرورة، وإذا لم ينعكس الأخص لم ينعكس الأعم، إذ لو انعكس الأعم لانعكس الأخص ، لأن لازم الأعم لازم الأخص ضرورة.
أقول : قد جرت العادة بتقديم عكس السوالب، لأن منها ما تنعكس كلية، والكلّي - وإن كان سلباً - يكون أشرف من الجزئي - وإن كان إيجاباً - لأنه أفيد في العلوم وأضبط.
فالسوالب إما كلّية وإما جزئية، فإن كانت كلية فسبع منها – وهي الوقتيتان، والوجوديتان، والممكنتان، والمطلقة العامة - لا تنعكس، لأن أخصها وهي الوقتية لا تنعكس، ومتى لم ينعكس الأخـص لم ينعكس الأعم.
أما أن الوقتية لا تنعكس : فلصدق قولنا : «لاشيء من القمر بمنخسف بالضرورة وقت التربيع لادائمـاً» مـع كـذب قولنـا بعـض المنخسف ليس بقمر بالإمكان العام الذي هو أعم الجهات، لأنّ كلّ منخسف فهو قمر بالضرورة.
وأما أنه إذا لم ينعكس الأخص لم ينعكس الأعم : فلأنه لو انعكس الأعم لانعكس الأخص؛ لأن العكس لازم الأعم، والأعم لازم الأخص، ولازم اللازم لازم.
واعلم أن معنى انعكاس القضية أنه يلزمها العكس لزوماً كلياً، فلايتبين ذلك بصدق العكس معها في مادة واحدة، بل يحتاج إلى برهان ينطبق على جميع المواد.
He said: As for negatives, if they are universal, then seven of them – namely the two temporal, the two existential, the two possible, and the general absolute – do not convert, due to the impossibility of conversion in the most specific of them – the temporal – because of the truth of our statement 'Necessarily, nothing of the moon is eclipsed at the time of quadrature, not always', and the falsehood of your statement 'Some eclipse is not a moon with general possibility which is the most general of modalities', because every eclipse is necessarily a moon. And if the specific does not convert, the general does not convert, because if the general converted, the specific would also convert, since the necessary concomitant of the general is a necessary concomitant of the specific necessarily. I say: The custom is to give priority to the conversion of negatives, because among them some convert universally, and the universal – even if negative – is nobler than the particular – even if affirmative – because it is more useful in the sciences and more precise. So negatives are either universal or particular. If they are universal, then seven of them – namely the two temporal, the two existential, the two possible, and the general absolute – do not convert, because the most specific of them, the temporal, does not convert, and whenever the specific does not convert, the general does not convert. As for the temporal not converting: due to the truth of our statement 'Nothing of the moon is eclipsed necessarily at the time of quadrature, not always' with the falsehood of our statement 'Some eclipse is not a moon with general possibility which is the most general of modalities', because every eclipse is necessarily a moon. And as for that if the specific does not convert the general does not convert: because if the general converted, the specific would also convert; because the converse is the necessary concomitant of the general, and the general is a necessary concomitant of the specific, and the necessary concomitant of a necessary concomitant is a necessary concomitant. And know that the meaning of a proposition's conversion is that the converse is implied by it with universal implication; this is not shown by the truth of the converse with it in one matter, but rather needs a proof that applies to all matters.
Source pages p. 345

Commentary
This passage discusses the conversion of universal negative propositions. The author states that seven specific types of universal negatives do not convert: the two temporal (necessary at a time, not always), the two existential (assertoric), the two possible, and the general absolute. The proof centers on the most specific type, the temporal (waqtiyya). An example: 'Necessarily, nothing of the moon is eclipsed at the time of quadrature, not always' is true, but its converse 'Some eclipse is not a moon with general possibility' is false, because every eclipse is necessarily a moon. Since the most specific does not convert, the more general types also cannot convert. The reasoning is a conditional argument: if the general converted, then the specific would also convert (because the converse is a necessary concomitant of the general, and the general is a necessary concomitant of the specific; the concomitant of a concomitant is a concomitant). The author also notes that universal negatives are given priority over particular affirmatives because they are more useful in the sciences. Finally, he emphasizes that conversion requires universal implication, not just a single true instance; a proof must cover all cases. For example, in Islamic law, the universal negative 'No wine is permissible' does not convert to 'No permissible is wine' because the converse would be false in the case of water, but the correct conversion of a simple universal negative (e.g., 'No human is stone' converts to 'No stone is human') must be proven by a general rule.

Key Terms
السوالب (al-sawālib): negative propositions
الوقتية (al-waqtiyya): temporal proposition
الوجوديتان (al-wujūdiyyatān): two existential propositions
الممكنتان (al-mumkinatān): two possible propositions
المطلقة العامة (al-muṭlaqa al-ʿāmma): general absolute proposition
لازم اللازم لازم (lāzim al-lāzim lāzim): the necessary concomitant of a necessary concomitant is necessary

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Because it is false in some instances.
B) Because its converse is false when using the general possibility modality.
C) Because all universal negatives do not convert.
D) Because the original proposition is false.

Key Takeaway
The conversion of universal negatives is not always valid; seven modal types fail conversion, proven by the temporal case and the principle that the necessary concomitant of a necessary concomitant is necessary.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
1) List the seven types of universal negative that do not convert as given in the text: two temporal, two existential, two possible, and the general absolute. For each type, think of a concrete proposition from an Islamic science (e.g., from fiqh or kalam) that would fall under that type.
?
2) Using the temporal example from the text (moon and eclipse), explain step by step why the temporal universal negative does not convert. Then, using the conditional proof (if the general converted, the specific would also convert), argue why the other six types also do not convert.

Why Seven Universal Negatives Do Not Convert
Author classifies universal negatives and explains non-conversion

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the principle that if the most specific universal negative does not convert, then more general ones also do not convert, using the time-limited negative as a test case.

Core Idea
The most specific universal negative determines the conversion rule for all more general ones.

In this passage, the author explains a key principle of immediate inference: not all universal negatives convert. By examining the most specific case—the time-limited negative—and showing its failure to convert, he proves that seven types of universal negatives are non-convertible. You will learn the logical reasoning behind this principle and how to use it to test conversion in modal and temporal propositions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the seven universal negatives
Locate the list in the Arabic: two time-limited (waqtiyya), two existential (wujūdiyya), two possible (mumkina), and the absolute general (muṭlaqa ʿāmma). Note that they are all universal negatives with modal or temporal qualifiers.
The author groups them by modality. The time-limited is the most specific because it restricts truth to a particular time.
2
Focus on the most specific: the time-limited
Understand why the time-limited negative is considered the most specific (akhass) among the seven. It is true only for a specific time, not always, making it narrower in scope than the others.
The principle relies on the logical relation between specific and general.
3
Analyze the counterexample for the time-limited
The author gives: 'No moon is eclipsed necessarily at the time of quadrature but not always' is true. But its converse (intended as 'No eclipsed thing is a moon' or similar) is false because 'Some eclipsed thing is not a moon' is false – every eclipsed thing is a moon. Verify the truth values.
The failure of the converse shows the original does not convert.
4
Apply the specific-to-general principle
The author argues: if the specific (time-limited) does not convert, then the general cannot convert. Because if the general converted, the specific would also convert (since conversion is a necessary concomitant of the general, and the general is a necessary concomitant of the specific).
This uses the modal principle: if A is necessary for B, and B is necessary for C, then A is necessary for C.
5
Distinguish proof for conversion vs. non-conversion
Note that to prove conversion requires a universal proof covering all materials, but to prove non-conversion, a single counterexample suffices. The author emphasizes this methodological point.
This is crucial for efficient reasoning: one counterexample disproves a universal claim.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فالسوالب إما كلية وإما جزئية، فإن كانت كلية فسبع منها – وهي الوقتيتان، والوجوديتان، والممكنتان، والمطلقة العامة - لا تنعكس، لأن أخصها وهي الوقتية لا تنعكس، ومتى لم ينعكس الأخـص لم ينعكس الأعم.
أما أن الوقتية لا تنعكس : فلصدق قولنا : «لاشيء من القمر بمنخسف بالضرورة وقت التربيع لادائمـاً» مـع كـذب قولنـا بعض المنخسف ليس بقمر بالإمكان العام الذي هو أعم الجهات، لأنّ كلّ منخسف فهو قمر بالضرورة.
وأما أنه إذا لم ينعكس الأخص لم ينعكس الأعم : فلأنه لو انعكس الأعم لانعكس الأخص؛ لأن العكس لازم الأعم، والأعم لازم الأخص، ولازم اللازم لازم.
واعلم أن معنى انعكاس القضية أنه يلزمها العكس لزوماً كلياً، فلايتبين ذلك بصدق العكس معها في مادة واحدة، بل يحتاج إلى برهان ينطبق على جميع المواد.
العكس المستوي
ومعنى عدم انعكاسها أنه ليس يلزمها العكس لزوماً كلياً، فيتضح ذلك بالتخلّف في مادة واحدة، فإنه لولزمها لزوماً كلياً لم يتخلف في شيء من المواد. فلهذا اكتفي في بيان عدم الانعكاس بمادة واحدة دون الانعكاس.
Negatives are either universal or particular. If universal, seven of them – namely the two time-limited, the two existential, the two possible, and the absolute general – do not convert, because their most specific, which is the time-limited, does not convert, and when the specific does not convert, the general does not convert. As for the time-limited not converting: because our statement 'Nothing of the moon is eclipsed necessarily at the time of quadrature but not always' is true while our statement 'Some eclipsed thing is not a moon by the general possibility which is the most general of the modes' is false, because every eclipsed thing is necessarily a moon. And as for when the specific does not convert the general does not convert: because if the general converted then the specific would convert; for the converse is a necessary concomitant of the general, and the general is a necessary concomitant of the specific, and the necessary concomitant of a necessary concomitant is necessary. Know that the meaning of the conversion of a proposition is that its converse follows it universally; so that is not shown by the truth of the converse with it in one material, but rather it needs a proof that applies to all materials. Simple conversion. And the meaning of its non-conversion is that its converse does not follow it universally; so that is made clear by failure in one material, because if it followed it universally it would not fail in any material. Hence one is content in demonstrating non-conversion with a single material, unlike conversion.
Source pages p. 346

Commentary
This passage is an explanatory commentary on the conversion of universal negative propositions. It begins by classifying negatives into universal and particular, then states that of the universal negatives, seven types do not convert: the two time-limited (waqtiyya), the two existential (wujūdiyya), the two possible (mumkina), and the absolute general (muṭlaqa ʿāmma). The reason given is that the most specific among them—the time-limited—does not convert, and by a logical principle, if the specific does not convert, the more general also does not convert. The passage then proves the non-conversion of the time-limited negative with an example: the proposition 'No moon is eclipsed necessarily at the time of quadrature but not always' is true, yet its converse 'Some eclipsed thing is not a moon by the general possibility' is false, because every eclipsed thing is necessarily a moon. Next, the passage demonstrates the principle that if the specific does not convert, the general does not, using a modal argument: conversion is a necessary concomitant (lāzim) of the general, and the general is a necessary concomitant of the specific, so if the general converted, the specific would also convert (since the necessary concomitant of a necessary concomitant is necessary). The passage concludes with a crucial methodological point: to prove that a proposition converts requires a universal proof applicable to all materials, but to prove non-conversion it suffices to show a single counterexample. This is because conversion is a universal logical consequence, so one exception disproves it. The heading 'Simple Conversion' (al-ʿaks al-mustawī) indicates the topic. This lesson is fundamental for understanding which negative propositions undergo immediate inference in Aristotelian logic as adapted by Islamic logicians.

Key Terms
السالبة الكلية
الوقتية
الأخص
الأعم
العكس

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
A universal negative with a time qualifier does not convert, and by the specific-to-general principle, this failure extends to all more general universal negatives.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Conversion of Necessary and Perpetual Negatives via Reductio
Author proves conversion of necessary and perpetual negatives via reductio.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to construct a reductio ad absurdum proof to verify the conversion of a necessary or perpetual universal negative proposition.

Core Idea
Necessary and perpetual negatives convert to universal perpetual negatives, proven by reducing the opposite to an impossibility.

In this lesson, we examine a key rule in modal logic: necessary and perpetual negative propositions convert to universal perpetual negatives. The proof uses a reductio argument: assuming the conversion is false leads to a self-contradiction, forcing us to accept the conversion as true. We will walk through each step of this elegant proof.

Guided Path
1
State the conversion rule
Identify the rule: if 'No J is B necessarily (or always)' is true, then 'No B is J always' must be true. Recognize that the converse is a universal perpetual negative.
The modality of the converse is always perpetual, even if the original is necessary.
2
Assume the converse is false
For reductio, assume the converse is false. Its contradictory, 'Some B is J by absolute generality' (particular affirmative without modality), is then true.
'Absolute generality' here means the proposition is asserted without any temporal or modal qualification.
3
Combine with the original premise
Take the assumed contradictory (Some B is J absolutely) and the original premise (No J is B necessarily/always). Form a syllogism: both premises are true under the assumption.
The original premise is assumed true; the contradictory is assumed true for the reductio.
4
Derive an impossible conclusion
From the two premises, infer 'Some B is not B' necessarily (or always). This is impossible because no thing can be not itself. The syllogism is valid (Festino: No J is B, Some B is J → Some B is not B).
The impossibility is a self-contradiction, not a formal fallacy.
5
Conclude the conversion holds
The impossibility arises only from the assumption that the converse is false. Since the original premise and the syllogistic combination are not at fault, the assumption must be false; therefore the converse is true.
This is a classic reductio ad absurdum: if the opposite leads to contradiction, the original must be accepted.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لخال وأمّا الضرورية والدائمة المطلقتان فينعكسان دائمة كلية، لأنه إذا صدق «بالضرورة - أودائماً - لاشيء من (ج) ب)، فيصدق دائماً لاشيء من (ب ج)» وإلا فـ «بعض (ب) (ج) بالإطلاق العام» وهو مع الأصل ينتج بعض (ب) ليس (ب) بالضرورة» في الضرورية و«دائماً» في الدائمة - وهو محال.
أقول : من السوالب الكلّية الضرورية المطلقة والدائمة المطلقة، وهما ينعكسان سالبة دائمة كلّيّة، لأنه إذا صدق «بالضرورة – أودائماً - لاشيء من (ج) ب)» وجب أن يصدق دائماً لاشيء من (ب ج)» وإلا لصدق نقيضه وهو «بعض (ب) (ج) بالإطلاق العام» وينضم إلى الأصل هكذا بعض (ب (ج) بالإطلاق، ولاشيء من (ج ب) بالضرورة – أو دائماً» ينتج بعض (ب) ليس (ب) بالضرورة» في الضرورية و«بالدوام» في الدائمة، وهو محال، و هذا المحال ليس بلازم من تركيب المقدمتين - لصحته - ولا من الأصل - لأنه مفروض الصدق – فتعين أن يكون لازماً من نقيض العكس، فيكون محالاً، فيكون العكس حقاً.
And as for the necessary and the perpetual in their absolute form, they convert to a universal perpetual negative; because if 'Necessarily or always: No J is B' is true, then 'Always: No B is J' is true. Otherwise, 'Some B is J by absolute generality', and that with the original yields 'Some B is not B necessarily' in the necessary, and 'always' in the perpetual—which is impossible. I say: Among universal negatives are the absolute necessary and the absolute perpetual; both convert to a universal perpetual negative; because if 'Necessarily or always: No J is B' is true, it must be that 'Always: No B is J' is true. Otherwise its contradictory would be true, namely 'Some B is J by absolute generality', and it is combined with the original thus: 'Some B is J by absolute generality, and No J is B necessarily or always' yields 'Some B is not B necessarily' in the necessary and 'perpetually' in the perpetual—which is impossible. And this impossibility is not a necessary consequence of the combination of the two premises—because they are true—nor from the original—because it is assumed true—so it must be a necessary consequence of the contradictory of the converse, so it is impossible, so the converse is true.
Source pages p. 347

Commentary
This passage provides a proof that two specific types of universal negative propositions—the necessary (darūriyya muṭlaqa) and the perpetual (dāʾima muṭlaqa)—convert to a universal perpetual negative. The author first states the rule: if 'Necessarily (or always) no J is B' is true, then 'Always no B is J' must be true. The proof uses reductio ad absurdum. Assume the conversion is false, i.e., the converse is not true; then its contradictory, 'Some B is J by absolute generality', is true. Combining this with the original premise 'No J is B necessarily (or always)' yields the conclusion 'Some B is not B necessarily (or always)'. This conclusion is impossible because it contradicts the identity principle (something cannot be not itself). The author carefully explains that the impossibility does not come from the premises themselves (since they are true) nor from their combination (which would be valid if the premises were true), but rather from the assumption that the converse is false. Therefore, the assumption is false, and the converse must be true. The argument uses a categorical syllogism in the first figure? Actually, it is a mixed syllogism: one premise is particular affirmative (some B is J) and one is universal negative (no J is B), which yields a particular negative conclusion (some B is not B) by Festino? But the conclusion is 'some B is not B', which is impossible because any B is itself. This shows that the conversion must hold. The lesson is a classic example of immediate inference with modal qualifiers in Islamic logic.

الدائمة المطلقة
الإطلاق العام
نقيض العكس

Necessary and perpetual universal negatives convert to universal perpetual negatives, proven by a reductio that shows assuming otherwise leads to an impossible self-contradiction.

Conversion of Necessary and Perpetual Universal Negatives
Author proves that necessary and perpetual universal negatives convert to perpetual universals, with objection and response.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine the correct conversion of a necessary or perpetual universal negative proposition and explain the proof by contradiction.

Core Idea
Necessary and perpetual universal negatives convert to perpetual universals, not to necessary ones.

In Islamic logic, conversion is a fundamental immediate inference. This lesson focuses on a nuanced case: universal negatives with modal qualifiers of necessity or perpetuity. The author argues that these propositions convert as perpetual universals, defends this with a proof by contradiction, and refutes an alternative view that necessary negatives convert as themselves. Understanding this rule sharpens your ability to handle modal propositions in logical reasoning.

Guided Path
1
State the conversion rule
Identify the claim: necessary and perpetual universal negatives (e.g., 'necessarily no J is B' or 'always no J is B') convert to perpetual universal negatives ('always no B is J').
The conversion changes the modality from necessary/perpetual to simply perpetual (dā'ima).
2
Follow the proof by contradiction
Assume the conversion is false, so its contradictory is true: 'some B is J' (absolute). Combine with the original premise to derive 'some B is not B' with the same modality as the premise. This is impossible because self-negation is only possible with an empty subject, but 'some B is J' ensures B is non-empty.
The proof relies on the impossibility of a thing being denied of itself under necessary/perpetual modality when the subject exists.
3
Analyze the objection and response
An objector suggests that 'some B is not B' could be true if B is non-existent. The response distinguishes two ways a negative can be true: due to subject non-existence or due to predicate negation. Since the contradictory of the conversion asserts the existence of some B, the subject is not empty, so the derived self-negation cannot be true in that way.
This step clarifies the conditions for truth of negative propositions.
4
Refute the alternative view
Some logicians held that necessary negatives convert as necessary. The author provides a counterexample: 'Zayd's mount is not a donkey necessarily' is true, but 'No donkey is Zayd's mount' is not necessary because donkeys can potentially be mounts. Thus the conversion is only perpetual.
This shows why the conversion modality must be perpetual, not necessary.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الضرورية والدائمة المطلقتان فينعكسان دائمة كلية، لأنه إذا صدق «بالضرورة - أودائماً - لاشيء من (ج) ب)، فيصدق دائماً لاشيء من (ب ج)» وإلا فـ «بعض (ب) (ج) بالإطلاق العام» وهو مع الأصل ينتج بعض (ب) ليس (ب) بالضرورة» في الضرورية و«دائماً» في الدائمة - وهو محال.
لا يقال : لانسلّم كذب قولنا : «بعض (ب) ليس (ب)» لجواز أن يكون الموضوع معدوماً، فيصدق سلبه عن نفسه.
لانا نقول : صدق السالبة إما لعدم موضوعها أو لوجوده مع سلب المحمول عنه، لكن الأول هاهنا منتف لوجود بعض (ب) حيث فرض صدق نقيض العكس، فلو صدق ذلك السلب لم يكن إلا لعدم المحمول وهو محال.
ومن الناس من ذهب إلى انعكاس السالبة الضرورية كنفسها، وهو فاسد - الجواز إمكان صفة لنوعين تثبت لأحدهما فقط بالفعل دون الآخر، فيكون النوع الآخر مسلوباً عما له تلك الصفة بالفعل بالضرورة مع إمكان ثبوت الصفة له، فلا يصدق سلبها عنه بالضرورة، كما أن مرکوب زید يكون ممكناً للفرس والحمار، ثابتاً للفرس بالفعل دون الحمار، فيصدق «لاشيء من مركوب زيد بحمار بالضرورة»، ولا يصدق.
As for the necessary and perpetual absolutes, they convert as perpetual universals, because if 'necessarily or always nothing of J is B' is true, then 'always nothing of B is J' is true; otherwise, 'some B is J by absolute generality' with the premise yields 'some B is not B necessarily' in the necessary and 'always' in the perpetual—which is impossible. It cannot be said: we do not admit the falsity of our statement 'some B is not B' because it is possible that the subject is non-existent, so its negation of itself is true. We reply: the truth of a negative is either due to the non-existence of its subject or due to its existence with negation of the predicate; but the first is absent here because of the existence of some B where the contradictory of the conversion is assumed true; if that negative were true, it would only be due to the non-existence of the predicate, which is impossible. Some people hold that the necessary negative converts as itself, which is invalid—because it is possible for a quality to be possible for two species, actually established for one of them alone, so the other species is necessarily negated of that which actually has that quality, while the quality can possibly be affirmed of it; thus its negation of it is not true necessarily, just as Zayd's mount is possible for horse and donkey, actually true for horse but not donkey, so 'Nothing of Zayd's mount is a donkey' is true necessarily, but it does not hold that...
Source pages p. 347

Commentary
This passage from the commentary on al-Shamsiyya discusses the conversion ('aks) of universal negative propositions that are either necessary (ḍarūriyya) or perpetual (dā'ima). The author states that both convert not as themselves but as perpetual universals (dā'ima kulliyya). The proof is by contradiction: assume the premise 'necessarily or always no J is B' is true, but the conversion 'always no B is J' is false. The falsity of the conversion implies its contradictory is true, i.e., 'some B is J' with absolute generality (bi-l-iṭlāq al-ʿāmm). Combining this with the original premise, we derive 'some B is not B' — necessarily if the premise was necessary, always if it was perpetual. This is impossible because a thing cannot be denied of itself under a necessary or perpetual modality (since self-negation is only possible when the subject is non-existent, but here some B exists by assumption). Therefore the conversion must be true. The author then addresses an objection: one might argue that 'some B is not B' could be true if B is non-existent, allowing the subject term to have no instances. The reply distinguishes two ways a negative can be true: either the subject has no instances (empty subject) or the subject exists and the predicate is denied of it. In this proof, the contradictory of the conversion ('some B is J') asserts the existence of some B, so the subject is not empty. Thus the derived 'some B is not B' must be false in that case, forcing the contradiction. Finally, the author refutes the view (attributed to some earlier logicians like Ibn Sīnā and Naṣīr al-Dīn al-Ṭūsī) that necessary negatives convert as necessary. He argues that it is possible for a quality to be possible for two species but actually present in only one. For example, 'mount of Zayd' can be a horse or a donkey; actually, Zayd rides a horse, so 'no mount of Zayd is a donkey' is true necessarily, but the conversion 'no donkey is a mount of Zayd' is not necessary because donkeys can potentially be mounts. Hence the conversion is perpetual, not necessary.

Converting Universal Negative Necessary Propositions
Refutes an objection against the conversion of necessary universal negative propositions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to convert a universal negative necessary proposition correctly, distinguishing the modality of the result.

Core Idea
A necessary universal negative converts to a possible universal negative, not a necessary one.

This passage defends the standard rule for converting universal negative necessary propositions against two challenges. The first challenge claims that a negative could be true if the subject is non-existent, undermining the conversion rule. The second mistaken view holds that the converted proposition retains necessity. Understanding the correct conversion is crucial for evaluating arguments in Islamic legal reasoning that depend on negation and modality.

Guided Path
1
Identify the original proposition
Locate the universal negative necessary proposition in the passage: e.g., 'No B is A necessarily'. Note its quality (negative), quantity (universal), and modality (necessity).
In the passage, the author uses the example 'Nothing of Zayd's mount is a donkey by necessity'.
2
Recall the conversion rule for universal negatives
For a simple universal negative ('No B is A'), the converse is also a universal negative ('No A is B'), preserving truth. But when modality is added, the rule must be refined.
The author refutes the view that the necessary negative converts as itself (i.e., remaining necessary).
3
Apply the modal conversion rule
The correct conversion of a universal negative necessary proposition is a universal negative possible proposition. Necessity does not carry over because the original predicate's negation may be possible for some individuals not actually possessing the property.
For example, 'Nothing of Zayd's mount is a donkey by necessity' converts to 'Nothing of the donkey is Zayd's mount by possibility'.
4
Understand why the objection fails
The objector claimed that a negative like 'Some B is not B' could be true if B is non-existent. The author replies that such a negative's truth could be due to subject non-existence or predicate negation. In conversion, the subject is assumed existent because the contradictory of the converse is true, so the negative cannot be true unless the predicate is non-existent—which is impossible.
This shows that the conversion rule is not undermined by non-existent subjects.
5
Test with the Zayd's mount example
Zayd's mount is a horse, not a donkey. So 'No mount of Zayd is a donkey by necessity' is true. Its converse would be 'No donkey is a mount of Zayd by necessity'? That is false because some donkey could possibly be Zayd's mount. Hence the converse must be possible, not necessary.
This concrete example illustrates why necessity does not convert.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لا يقال : لانسلّم كذب قولنا : «بعض (ب) ليس (ب)» لجواز أن يكون الموضوع معدوماً، فيصدق سلبه عن نفسه.
لانا نقول : صدق السالبة إما لعدم موضوعها أو لوجوده مع سلب المحمول عنه، لكن الأول هاهنا منتف لوجود بعض (ب) حيث فرض صدق نقيض العكس، فلو صدق ذلك السلب لم يكن إلا لعدم المحمول وهو محال.
ومن الناس من ذهب إلى انعكاس السالبة الضرورية كنفسها، وهو فاسد - الجواز إمكان صفة لنوعين تثبت لأحدهما فقط بالفعل دون الآخر، فيكون النوع الآخر مسلوباً عما له تلك الصفة بالفعل بالضرورة مع إمكان ثبوت الصفة له، فلا يصدق سلبها عنه بالضرورة، كما أن مرکوب زید يكون ممكناً للفرس والحمار ، ثابتاً للفرس بالفعل دون الحمار، فيصدق «لاشيء من مركوب زيد بحمار بالضرورة»، ولا يصدق لاشيء من الحمار بمركوب زيد بالضرورة لصدق نقيضه، وهو : بعض الحمار مركوب زيد بالإمكان.
It is not said: We do not accept the falsity of our statement 'Some B is not B' because the subject may be non-existent, so its negation of itself is true. We say: The truth of a negative is either due to the non-existence of its subject or its existence with the negation of the predicate, but the first is here negated because some B exists given the assumption of the truth of the contradictory of the conversion. So if that negative were true, it would only be due to the non-existence of the predicate, which is impossible. Some people have held that the necessary negative converts as itself, but it is false – because it is possible for a property to belong to two species, actually existing in only one of them, so the other species is necessarily denied of that which actually has the property, while it is possible for the property to belong to it. Thus, 'Nothing of Zayd's mount is a donkey by necessity' is true, but 'Nothing of the donkey is Zayd's mount by necessity' is not true because its contradictory is true: 'Some donkey is Zayd's mount by possibility.'
Source pages p. 348

Commentary
This passage defends the standard position on the conversion of universal negative necessary propositions against an objection. The objector argues that the conversion rule fails because a negative proposition like 'Some B is not B' could be true if the subject is non-existent. The author replies that such a negative's truth could be due to the subject's non-existence or the predicate's negation, but in the context of conversion, the subject exists (since the contradictory of the conversion is assumed true), so the only way the negative could be true is if the predicate is non-existent—which is impossible. The author then refutes a different mistaken view that the necessary negative converts as itself (i.e., remains necessary). Using an example: Zayd's mount is possibly a horse or donkey, but actually a horse. So 'Nothing of Zayd's mount is a donkey by necessity' is true, but 'Nothing of the donkey is Zayd's mount by necessity' is false because its contradictory 'Some donkey is Zayd's mount by possibility' is true. Thus the conversion of a necessary negative must be a possible negative, not a necessary one.

Conversion of Conditional and Customary Universal Negative Propositions
States conversion rules for conditional and customary universal negative propositions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the conversion rules for conditional and customary universal negative propositions to Islamic logical arguments.

Core Idea
Conditional negatives convert to customary, not permanent, universals.

This passage explains how conditional ('mushtarata') and customary ('urfiyya') universal negative propositions undergo immediate inference (conversion). The author demonstrates that they convert to customary universal negatives, not permanent ones, using a proof by contradiction. Understanding this rule is essential for correctly evaluating arguments in Islamic sciences that rely on conditional or customary premises, such as those found in usul al-fiqh and kalam.

Guided Path
1
Identify the proposition type
Determine whether the proposition is conditional (mushtarata) or customary ('urfiyya) and whether it is universal or particular. The passage focuses on universals first.
Conditional negatives include phrases like 'by necessity' or 'always'; customary ones are usually true under normal circumstances.
2
State the conversion rule
Both conditional and customary universal negatives convert to a customary universal negative that is not permanent (la da'ima). So the result is 'urfiyya 'amma la da'ima.
The converted proposition asserts that nothing of the predicate is the subject as long as it is the predicate, as a matter of custom.
3
Understand the proof by contradiction
Assume the conversion is false: i.e., sometime some B is J when it is B. Combine with the original 'nothing of J is B as long as J' to deduce 'some B is not B when it is B', which is impossible. Hence the conversion must be true.
This proof shows the necessity of the conversion rule.
4
Apply to particular propositions
Particular conditional and customary negatives also convert to customary universal but not permanent in the particular sense. The reasoning is analogous, using the fact that the customary universal is necessary for the two universal types.
The same contradiction method applies if the conversion were false.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما المشروطة والعرفية العامتان فتنعكسان عرفية عامة لادائمة، لأنه إذا صدق «بالضرورة - أودائماً - لاشيء من (ج ب) مادام (ج)» ف دائماً لاشيء من (ب (ج) مادام (ب)» وإلا فبعض (ب ج) حين هو (ب)» وهو مع الأصل ينتج بعض (ب) ليس (ب) حين هو (ب)» وهو محال. وأما المشروطة والعرقية الخاصتان فتنعكسان عرفية عامة لادائمة في البعض: أما العرفية العامة فلكونها لازمة للعامتين. وأما اللادوام في البعض فلاله لو كذب بعض (ب ج) بالإطلاق العام لصدق «لاشيء من (ب (ج) دائماً»، فينعكس إلى «لاشيء مـن (ج ب) دائماً»، و قد كان كل رج ب) بالفعل» - هذا خلف. القول : السالبة الكلية المشروطة والعرفية العامتان تنعكسان عرفية عامة كلية، لأنه متى صدق بالضرورة – أودائماً – لاشيء من ( ج ب) مادام (ج) » صدق دائماً لاشيء من (ب) ج) مادام (ب)» وإلا فـ«بعض (ب ج) حين هو (ب) » لأنّه نقيضه، ونضمه مع الأصل بأن نقول : بعض (ب ج) حين هو (ب) وبالضرورة أو دائماً لاشيء من (ج ب) مادام (ج) » فينتج : «بعض (ب) ليس (ب) حين هو (ب) وإنه محال وهو ناشئ من نقيض العكس، فالعكس حق.
As for the conditional and customary universals, they convert as customary universal, not permanent. Because if 'By necessity – or always – nothing of J is B as long as J' is true, then always nothing of B is J as long as B. Otherwise, some B is J when it is B, which with the original yields some B is not B when it is B, which is impossible. As for the conditional and customary particulars, they convert as customary universal, not permanent in the particular: The customary universal because it is necessary for the two universals. As for the non-permanence in the particular, because if some B is J absolutely were false, then 'Nothing of B is J always' would be true, which converts to 'Nothing of J is B always', whereas every J is B actually – this is a contradiction. The statement: The universal negative conditional and customary universals convert as universal customary, because whenever 'By necessity – or always – nothing of J is B as long as J' is true, then always nothing of B is J as long as B, otherwise some B is J when it is B, because it is its contradictory, and we join it with the original saying: Some B is J when it is B, and by necessity or always nothing of J is B as long as J, so it results: Some B is not B when it is B, which is impossible, and it arises from the contradictory of the conversion, so the conversion is true.

Commentary
This passage defines the conversion rules for conditional (mushtarata) and customary ('urfiyya) negative propositions. It explains that both the universal conditional and universal customary negatives convert to customary universal negatives, not permanent (da'ima) ones. The proof is by contradiction: if the conversion were false, a contradiction arises. For particular conditional and customary negatives, they convert to customary universal but not permanent in the particular sense. The reasoning uses the fact that the customary universal is necessary for the two universal types. The author reinforces the rule by showing that denying the conversion leads to 'some B is not B', which is impossible. All examples use the logical variables J and B as placeholders.

Conversion Rule for Modal Universal Negatives
Author states conversion rule for two modal universal negatives.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conversion rule for general conditioned and general customary universal negatives and apply it to modal propositions from Islamic theology.

Core Idea
Modality shifts from necessity to custom during conversion of these proposition types.

In Islamic logic, immediate inferences like conversion ('aks) are essential for valid argumentation. This passage addresses a subtle case: universal negative propositions with modal conditions. The rule states that both 'conditioned' (mashruta) and 'general customary' (urfiyya amma) universal negatives convert to a weaker general customary universal negative. We'll unpack the rule and its rationale.

Guided Path
1
Identify the proposition types
Recognize that the passage discusses two types: 'conditioned' (mashruta) where the negative holds with necessity under the condition of being J, and 'general customary' (urfiyya amma) where it holds perpetually but without necessity.
The condition 'as long as it is J' is crucial.
2
State the conversion rule
The rule says both types convert to a 'general customary universal negative' with the condition changed to the new subject. That is: 'No J is B as long as it is J' becomes 'No B is J as long as it is B', but with customary modality.
The modality weakens from necessity/perpetuity to custom.
3
Test the rule on an example
Take a concrete example: 'No animal is rational as long as it is an animal' (conditioned). Its converse according to the rule: 'No rational thing is an animal as long as it is rational' (customary). Check if this seems plausible.
This shows how the subject condition transfers.
4
Distinguish from simple conversion
Compare with the simple conversion of 'No J is B' (without condition) which converts to 'No B is J' with the same modality. Here the condition and modality change, so the rule is specific to these modal types.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القول : السالبة الكلية المشروطة والعرفية العامتان تنعكسان عرفية عامة كلية، لأنه متى صدق بالضرورة – أودائماً – لاشيء من ( ج ب) مادام (ج) » صدق دائماً لاشيء من (ب) ج) مادام (ب)» وإلا فـ«بعض (ب ج) حين هو (ب) » لأنّه نقيضه، ونضمه مع الأصل بأن نقول : بعض (ب ج) حين هو (ب) وبالضرورة أو دائماً لاشيء من (ج ب) مادام (ج) » فينتج : «بعض (ب) ليس (ب) حين هو (ب) وإنه محال وهو ناشئ من نقيض العكس، فالعكس حق.
Statement: The universal negative conditioned and general customary (propositions) are converted to a general customary universal. Because when it is true 'by necessity – or always – nothing of J is B as long as it is J', then it is always true 'nothing of B is J as long as it is B'. Otherwise, then 'some B is J while it is B' is its contradictory, and we conjoin it with the original by saying: some B is J while it is B, and by necessity or always nothing of J is B as long as it is J, so it produces 'some B is not B while it is B', which is impossible. So the conversion is correct.
Source pages p. 349

Commentary
This passage explains the conversion rule for universal negative propositions of two modal types: the 'conditioned' (mashruta) where the necessity is tied to the subject's description, and the 'general customary' (urfiyya amma) which is a weaker modality. The author states that both convert to a general customary universal (i.e., a universal negative with the subject condition 'as long as it is B' but without necessity or perpetuity). The proof is by contradiction: assume the converse is false, so its contradictory (some B is J while it is B) is true. Combining this with the original premise yields a syllogism that concludes some B is not B while it is B, which is impossible. Hence the assumption fails, proving the conversion.

Key Terms

المشروطة
العرفية العامة
العكس

D) A special customary universal negative

Key Takeaway
General conditioned and general customary universal negatives both convert to a general customary universal negative, with a weakening of modality.

Proof by Contradiction for the Conversion Rule
Author proves conversion rule via reduction to absurdity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the proof by contradiction that validates the conversion rule for modal universal negatives.

Core Idea
The conversion is proven by assuming its falsehood leads to an absurd conclusion.

How do we know the conversion rule is sound? The author provides a tight proof by contradiction. By assuming the converse is false, we derive a contradiction: some B is not B. This lesson walks through the steps of this argument, showing how a valid syllogism yields an impossibility, thereby confirming the rule.

Guided Path
1
Assume the converse false
The proof begins by assuming the opposite: that the converse is not true. For a universal negative, its falsehood implies the truth of its contradictory, which is a particular affirmative.
The contradictory of 'No B is J as long as it is B' is 'Some B is J while it is B'.
2
Combine with original premise
Take the original premise: 'No J is B as long as it is J' (with necessity or perpetuity). This and the assumed contradictory form two premises of a syllogism.
The original premise is the major, the assumed contradictory is the minor.
3
Derive the conclusion
From 'Some B is J while it is B' and 'No J is B as long as it is J', we infer 'Some B is not B while it is B'. This follows by a valid categorical syllogism (Ferio).
The conclusion says something is not itself, which is impossible.
4
Recognize the contradiction
The conclusion 'some B is not B' is absurd. Since the reasoning is valid, the only source of falsehood must be the assumption that the converse is false. Therefore, the converse must be true.
This proves the conversion rule.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Statement: The universal negative conditioned and general customary (propositions) are converted to a general customary universal. Because when it is true 'by necessity – or always – nothing of J is B as long as it is J', then it is always true 'nothing of B is J as long as it is B'. Otherwise, then 'some B is J while it is B' is its contradictory, and we conjoin it with the original by saying: some B is J while it is B, and by necessity or always nothing of J is B as long as it is J, so it produces 'some B is not B while it is B', which is impossible. So the conversion is correct.

Commentary

The author states that both convert to a general customary universal (i.e., a universal negative with the subject condition 'as long as it is B' but without necessity or perpetuity). Combining this with the original premise yields a syllogism that concludes some B is not B while it is B, which is impossible. Hence the assumption fails, proving the conversion.

Why the General Conditioned Does Not Convert to Itself
Author refutes a claim about general conditioned conversion

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify why the general conditioned universal negative cannot convert to itself by analysing the role of the subject's description in the necessity.

Core Idea
The subject's description is baked into the necessity, so its converse changes the condition, breaking entailment.

This lesson tackles a common mistake: claiming that the general conditioned (mashruta amma) universal negative converts to itself. The commentator Qutbi shows why this is false by examining meaning and entailment. We'll walk through his reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Identify the original proposition
The original is a general conditioned universal negative: 'Nothing of J is B by necessity as long as it is J'. The necessity is tied to the subject's description (being J).
2
Recognise the role of the subject's description
In a general conditioned proposition, the necessity holds only as long as the subject has that description. The meaning involves the incompatibility of the predicate description with the combination of the subject's description and its essence.
3
Formulate the purported converse
The mistaken claim is that the converse is also a general conditioned: 'Nothing of B is J by necessity as long as it is B'. The necessity would then be tied to the new subject's description (being B).
4
Compare the conditions of original and converse
The original says: the predicate's description (B) is incompatible with the subject being J and its essence. The converse says: the subject's description (J) is incompatible with the predicate being B and its essence. These are different conditions.
5
Check entailment
The first condition does not entail the second. For example, something could be J and not B necessarily while being J, but it could also be B and be J while being B, because the necessity condition changes. The author states 'it is clear that the first does not entail the second'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
شرح الرسالة الشمسية ومنهم من زعم أن المشروطة العامة تنعكس كنفسها، وهو باطل، لأن المشروطة العامة هي التي لوصف الموضوع فيها دخل في تحقق الضرورة على ماسبق، فيكون مفهوم السالبة المشروطة العامة منافاة وصف المحمول لمجموع وصف الموضوع وذاته، ومفهوم عكسها منافاة وصف الموضوع المجموع وصف المحمول وذاته، ومن البين أن الأول لا يستلزم الثاني. (ج) وأما المشروطة والعرقية الخاصتان فتنعكسان عرفية عامة مقيدة باللادوام في البعض، فإنه إذا صدق بالضرورة – أودائماً – لا شيء من ج ب) مادام (ج) لادائماً » فليصدق دائماً لاشيء من (ب ج) مادام (ب) لادائماً في البعض - أي بعض (ب) ج) بالفعل - فإن اللادوام في القضايا الكلية مطلقة عامة كلّيّة على ما عرفت، وإذا قيد بالبعض تكون مطلقة عامة جزئية. أما صدق العرفية العامة وهي لاشيء من (ب (ج) مادام (ب) فلأنها لازمة للعامتين، ولازم العام لازم الخاص. وأما صدق اللادوام في البعض : فلأنه لولم يصدق «بعض (ب ج) بالفعل» لصدق لا شيء من (ب) (ج) دائماً»، وتنعكس إلى «لاشيء من (ج) (ب) دائماً»، وقد كان بحكم اللادوام الأصل «كل (ج ب) بالفعل» هذا خلف. وأنما لا تنعكسان إلى العرفية العامة المقيدة باللادوام في الكل، لأنه 1) الاختلاف هنا مثل ما مضى من الاختلاف في عكس السالبة الكلية الضرورية .
Commentary on al-Risala al-Shamsiyya: And among them is one who claims that the general conditioned (universal negative) converts to itself, and this is false. Because the general conditioned proposition is one where the description of the subject enters into the realization of necessity, as previously. So the meaning of the universal negative conditioned general is the incompatibility of the description of the predicate with the combination of the description of the subject and its essence. And the meaning of its converse is the incompatibility of the description of the subject with the combination of the description of the predicate and its essence. And it is clear that the first does not entail the second. (J) And as for the special conditioned and special customary (universal negatives), they convert to a general customary universal restricted by non-perpetuity in some. Because when it is true 'by necessity – or always – nothing of J is B while it is J not always', then let it be true 'always nothing of B is J while it is B not always in some' – i.e., some B is J actually. For the non-perpetuity in universal propositions is an absolute general universal as you have known, and when restricted by 'some' it becomes an absolute general particular. As for the truth of the general customary, which is 'nothing of B is J while it is B', it is because it is necessary for the two general (propositions), and what is necessary for the general is necessary for the particular. And as for the truth of the non-perpetuity in some, it is because if 'some B is J actually' were not true, then 'nothing of B is J always' would be true, and it converts to 'nothing of J is B always', and according to the original non-perpetuity 'every J is B actually' – this is a contradiction. And they do not convert to the general customary restricted by non-perpetuity in all, because the difference here is like what has passed regarding the difference in conversion of the necessary universal negative.
Source pages p. 350

Commentary
This passage from the commentary (sharh) of Qutbi on al-Risala al-Shamsiyya first refutes a mistaken view that the general conditioned (mashruta amma) universal negative converts to itself (i.e., to another general conditioned). The author explains that because the original involves the subject's description in the necessity, the converse's description condition is different, so entailment fails. Then the passage discusses the special conditioned and special customary (mashruta khassa and urfiyya khassa) universal negatives. These convert to a general customary universal but with a qualification of non-perpetuity (ladawam) in some (i.e., the converse is a universal negative with a particular absolute component). The truth of the general customary part is justified by its being a consequence of the original general propositions. The non-perpetuity in some is proven by a contradiction: if no B were actually J, then the converse would imply the original's opposite, contradicting the given non-perpetuity. Finally, it notes that they do not convert to a general customary restricted by non-perpetuity in all, referring to an earlier discussion on the conversion of necessary universal negatives.

Key Terms
المشروطة العامة
السالبة الكلية المشروطة العامة

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Because the original is not a universal proposition.
B) Because the original's necessity is tied to the subject's description, and the converse changes the description condition.
C) Because conversion only works for absolute propositions.
D) Because the original contains a particular affirmative component.

Key Takeaway
A general conditioned universal negative does not convert to itself because the necessity is tied to the subject's description, and the converse changes the subject, altering the condition.

The Conversion of Special Conditioned and Special Customary Negatives
Author explains conversion of special conditioned and customary negatives

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to construct the correct converse of a special conditioned or special customary universal negative and justify its components.

Core Idea
These negatives convert to a general customary with a non-perpetuity clause in some — a weaker form.

The special conditioned (mashruta khassa) and special customary (urfiyya khassa) universal negatives have a more complex conversion than the necessary negatives. The commentator explains that they become a general customary (urfiyya amma) universal negative restricted by non-perpetuity (ladawam) in some (i.e., some instances are actually affirmative). We'll learn why this is correct and why they don't convert to a general customary without restriction or with non-perpetuity in all.

Guided Path
1
Identify the original proposition
The original is a special conditioned or special customary universal negative, e.g., 'Nothing of J is B by necessity (or always) while it is J, but not always' – meaning it is true for all J that are B, but not perpetually; there is an exception.
The non-perpetuity (ladawam) indicates that not always is the proposition true; i.e., some J is B actually.
2
State the converse result
The correct converse is: 'Always nothing of B is J while it is B, but not always in some' – i.e., a general customary universal negative (nothing of B is J while it is B) with the addition that some B is J actually (the non-perpetuity in some).
3
Decompose the converse into two parts
The converse has two components: (a) the general customary part: 'Nothing of B is J while it is B' – a universal negative with a temporal condition (as long as it is B). (b) the non-perpetuity in some: 'Some B is J actually' – a particular affirmative absolute proposition.
The general customary part is the main conversion; the non-perpetuity part is an additional qualification.
4
Verify the general customary part
The general customary part is necessary from the original because it is a consequence of the two general propositions (the special conditioned and special customary imply their general counterparts). 'What is necessary for the general is necessary for the particular.'
5
Verify the non-perpetuity in some by contradiction
If no B were actually J (i.e., the exception were false), then 'Nothing of B is J always' would be true (i.e., perpetual). This would convert by standard conversion to 'Nothing of J is B always'. But the original says not always (non-perpetuity) – contradiction. Thus, some B must be J actually.

Source Text

Why the General Conditional Does Not Convert as Itself
Author refutes a claim about general conditional conversion

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the logical structure of a general conditional and explain why it does not convert to itself.

Core Idea
The general conditional’s necessity is tied to the subject’s description, making conversion non-trivially different.

The author refutes a claim that the general conditional (مشروطة عامة) converts to itself. Understanding why reveals how modal conditions affect immediate inference. This lesson breaks down the logical structure and the reason for the invalidity.

Guided Path
1
Define the general conditional
Recall that in a general conditional, the predicate's description enters into the necessity of the judgment. This means the necessity is conditional on the subject having a certain description.
The definition was given earlier in the text: ضرورة محمول لموضوع بشرط وصفه.
2
Write the meaning of a negative general conditional
A negative general conditional states the incompatibility of the predicate's description with the combination of the subject's description and its essence. For example, 'No J is B as long as J is not always' involves necessity conditional on J's non-permanence.
Use schematic variables: سلب المشروطة العامة يعني منافاة وصف المحمول لمجموع وصف الموضوع وذاته.
3
Write the meaning of the converse
The converse would be 'No B is J as long as B is not always', which means the incompatibility of the subject's description with the combination of the predicate's description and its essence.
هذا هو عكس القضية.
4
Compare the two meanings
The first involves the predicate description negated relative to the subject+essence. The second involves the subject description negated relative to the predicate+essence. These are different logical relations; the first does not entail the second.
The author states: من البين أن الأول لا يستلزم الثاني.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومنهم من زعم أن المشروطة العامة تنعكس كنفسها، وهو باطل، لأن المشروطة العامة هي التي لوصف الموضوع فيها دخل في تحقق الضرورة على ماسبق، فيكون مفهوم السالبة المشروطة العامة منافاة وصف المحمول لمجموع وصف الموضوع وذاته، ومفهوم عكسها منافاة وصف الموضوع المجموع وصف المحمول وذاته، ومن البين أن الأول لا يستلزم الثاني. (ج) وأما المشروطة والعرقية الخاصتان فتنعكسان عرفية عامة مقيدة باللادوام في البعض، فإنه إذا صدق بالضرورة – أودائماً – لا شيء من ج ب) مادام (ج) لادائماً » فليصدق دائماً لاشيء من (ب ج) مادام (ب) لادائماً في البعض - أي بعض (ب) ج) بالفعل - فإن اللادوام في القضايا الكلية مطلقة عامة كلّيّة على ما عرفت، وإذا قيد بالبعض تكون مطلقة عامة جزئية. أما صدق العرفية العامة وهي لاشيء من (ب (ج) مادام (ب) فلأنها لازمة للعامتين، ولازم العام لازم الخاص. وأما صدق اللادوام في البعض : فلأنه لولم يصدق «بعض (ب ج) بالفعل» لصدق لا شيء من (ب) (ج) دائماً»، وتنعكس إلى «لاشيء من (ج) (ب) دائماً»، وقد كان بحكم اللادوام الأصل «كل (ج ب) بالفعل» هذا خلف. وأنما لا تنعكسان إلى العرفية العامة المقيدة باللادوام في الكل، لأنه 1) الاختلاف هنا مثل ما مضى من الاختلاف في عكس السالبة الكلية الضرورية . العكس المستوي يصدق «لاشيء من الكاتب بساكن مادام كاتباً لا دائماً، ويكـذب لاشيء من الساكن بكاتب مادام ساكناً لا دائماً» لكذب اللادوام، وهو كل ساكن كاتب باللإطلاق العام لصدق بعض الساكن ليس بكاتب دائماً» لأن من الساكن ما هو ساكن دائماً - كالأرض.
Among them is one who claimed that the general conditional converts as itself, but this is false. Because the general conditional is that in which the predicate's description enters into the realization of necessity as previously mentioned. Thus the meaning of the negative general conditional is the incompatibility of the predicate's description with the combination of the subject's description and its essence, and the meaning of its converse is the incompatibility of the subject's description with the combination of the predicate's description and its essence, and it is clear that the first does not entail the second. (J) As for the particular conditional and the particular 'urfiya, they convert to a general 'urfiya restricted by non-permanence in some. For if it is true necessarily — or always — 'No J is B as long as J is not always' then let it be true always 'No B is J as long as B is not always in some' — i.e., some B is J actually. For non-permanence in universal propositions is absolute general universal as you have known, and if restricted to some it becomes absolute general particular. As for the truth of the general 'urfiya, which is 'No B is J as long as B', it is because it follows from the two general [propositions], and what follows the general follows the particular. As for the truth of non-permanence in some: because if 'Some B is J actually' were not true, then 'No B is J always' would be true, and it converts to 'No J is B always', while by the original non-permanence it is 'Every J is B actually' — this is a contradiction. And they do not convert to the general 'urfiya restricted by non-permanence in all, because the difference here is like the previous difference in the conversion of the necessary universal negative. The simple conversion: 'No writer is stationary as long as he writes not always' is true, and 'No stationary is a writer as long as he is stationary not always' is false due to the falsehood of non-permanence, i.e., 'Every stationary is a writer with absolute generality' because it is true that 'Some stationary is not a writer always' because among the stationary there is what is always stationary, like the earth.
Source pages p. 350

Commentary
This passage defines the general conditional (مشروطة عامة) and explains its conversion property. The author begins by refuting a false claim that the general conditional converts as itself (i.e., into another general conditional). He argues that the meaning of the negative general conditional involves the negation of the predicate relative to the combination of the subject's description and its essence. The converse would involve the opposite order—negation of the subject relative to the combination of the predicate description and its essence. These are not logically equivalent, so the claim is false. Next, the author turns to particular conditionals (مشروطة خاصة) and particular 'urfiya (عرقية خاصة). He states that they convert to a general 'urfiya (عرفية عامة) restricted by non-permanence (لادوام) in some individuals. He provides a proof: if 'No J is B as long as J is not always' is true (with necessity or always), then we can infer that 'No B is J as long as B is not always' must be true for some B actually J. The non-permanence in universal propositions yields a general absolute proposition; when restricted to some, it becomes particular absolute. The truth of the general 'urfiya follows because it is a logical consequence of the two general propositions, and what follows the general also follows the particular. The truth of the non-permanence part is proven by contradiction: if no B were actually J, then 'No B is J always' would be true, which converts to 'No J is B always', contradicting the original non-permanence that assumed 'Every J is B actually'. Finally, the author explains why these conditionals do not convert to a general 'urfiya restricted by non-permanence in all individuals. He gives an example: it is true that 'No writer is stationary as long as he is a writer'—but only 'not always'; yet its converse 'No stationary is a writer as long as it is stationary not always' is false because the non-permanence condition fails—there are stationary things (like the earth) that are always stationary, so not every stationary is a writer at some time. Thus, the conversion holds only with non-permanence in some, not in all.

العكس المستوي
السالبة الكلية

Conversion of Particular Conditionals to General 'Urfiya with Partial Non-Permanence
Author explains conversion rules for particular conditionals

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the conversion rule for particular conditionals and particular 'urfiya, and verify the conditions for the converse.

Core Idea
Particular conditionals convert to a general 'urfiya restricted by non-permanence in some, not all, individuals.

The author explains that the particular conditional and particular 'urfiya do not convert to themselves but to a different type of proposition. Understanding the proof and the restriction to partial non-permanence is essential for valid inference.

Guided Path
1
Understand the original proposition
The original is a particular conditional or particular 'urfiya. The passage uses the form: 'No J is B as long as J is not always' (with necessity or always). This is a particular conditional because 'not always' restricts the scope.
Original: لا شيء من ج ب مادام ج ليس دائماً (بالضرورة أو دائماً)
2
Identify the intended converse
The converse is: 'No B is J as long as B is not always in some' — i.e., a general 'urfiya (لا شيء من ب ج مادام ب) restricted by non-permanence in some (that some B is J actually).
Converse: عرفية عامة مقيدة باللادوام في البعض.
3
Prove the general 'urfiya part
The general 'urfiya part (No B is J as long as B) is a logical consequence of the original and the following: the original implies some universal propositions that entail the general 'urfiya. The author says it is 'lazim' for the two generals, and what is necessary for the general is necessary for the particular.
أما صدق العرفية العامة فلأنها لازمة للعامتين، ولازم العام لازم الخاص.
4
Prove the non-permanence part by contradiction
Assume no B is J actually (i.e., 'some B is J actually' is false). Then 'No B is J always' would be true. This converts to 'No J is B always', which contradicts the original proposition that 'Every J is B actually' (implied by non-permanence).
البرهان بالخلف: لو لم يصدق بعض (ب) (ج) بالفعل لصدق لا شيء من (ب) (ج) دائماً، وينعكس إلى لا شيء من (ج) (ب) دائماً، وهذا خلف.
5
Explain why non-permanence only in some, not all
The conversion does not hold with non-permanence in all because of counterexamples like the earth: 'No writer is stationary as long as he is a writer (not always)' is true, but its converse with non-permanence in all would be false because some stationary things (e.g., the earth) are always stationary.
مثال: العكس المستوي يصدق في الكاتب والساكن مع اللادوام في البعض، لا في الكل.
