Conversion of Partial Negative Conditionals
Author explains conversion rules for partial negative specific conditionals.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the conversion rule for partial negative specific conditionals to determine which modal conditionals convert and which do not, using the proof by contradiction and the subordination principle.

Core Idea
Only specific conditional and specific 'urfiyya partial negatives convert — to a specific 'urfiyya; others do not.

In this lesson, we examine the conversion of partial negative conditionals that carry a modal condition like 'as long as not always'. The author first proves that one type (specific conditional and specific 'urfiyya) does convert, using a reductio argument. He then shows that other types, such as necessary and temporal conditionals, do not convert because their converses under absolute possibility are false. Understanding this helps us navigate modal inferences in Islamic logical arguments.

Guided Path
1
Identify the proposition types
The passage deals with partial negative propositions that are 'specific conditional' (مشروطة خاصة) and 'specific ‘urfiyya' (عرقية خاصة), having the form 'Some J is not B as long as J is not always'. Recognize this form in the text.
These are distinct from necessary or temporal conditionals.
2
Follow the conversion proof
Assume the original 'Some J is not B as long as J is not always' is true. Pick an individual D that is J actually. By the non-permanence condition, D is also B. Assume for contradiction that D is J as long as B, which would imply B when J, contradicting the original. Hence D is not J as long as B, so the converse 'Some B is not J as long as B is not always' holds.
The proof uses a reductio ad absurdum (خلف) with a witness individual D.
3
State the successful conversion
The conversion of a specific conditional or specific 'urfiyya partial negative yields a specific 'urfiyya: 'Some B is not J as long as B is not always'.
This is the only valid conversion for this class of modal conditionals.
4
Identify non-converting types
Necessary and temporal partial negatives do not convert. Example: 'Some animal is not human' is necessarily true, but its converse 'Some human is not animal' under absolute possibility is false. Similarly, the temporal example 'Some moon is not eclipsed at quadrature not always' also fails.
The author provides these as counterexamples.
5
Apply the subordination principle
Since necessity is the most specific simple modality and temporal the most specific composite, and these do not convert, no weaker modality can convert. If any weaker modality had a valid conversion, the stronger ones would also convert (by subordination). Hence, no other modal conditionals convert.
This general rule is derived from the failure of the strongest modalities.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[۷۹- عكس السوالب الجزئية المشروطة والعرقية الخاصتين] لقال: وإن كانت جزئية فالمشروطة والعرقية الخاصتان تنعكسان عرقية خاصة، لأنه إذا صدق بالضرورة - أو دائماً - بعض (ج) ليس (ب) مادام لا دائماً» صدق دائماً ليس بعض (ب) ج) مادام (ب) لادائماً» لأن نفرض ذات الموضوع وهو (ج) (د) ، فـرد (ج) بالفعل و(د ب أيضاً بحكم اللادوام، وليس (د) (ج) مادام (ب) وإلا لكان (د ج) حين هو (ب)، فـ(ب) حين هو (ج)؛ وقد كان ليس (ب) مادام (ج) - هذا خلف. وإذا صدق (ج) و (ب) على (د) وتنافيا فيه صدق «بعض (ب) ليس (ج) مادام (ب) لادائماً» – وهو المطلوب. وأما البواقي فلا تنعكس لأنه يصدق بالضرورة بعض الحيوان ليس إنسان) و «بالضرورة ليس بعض القمر بمنخسف وقت التربيع لادائماً» مع كذب عكسها بالإمكان العام الذي هو أعمّ الجهات، لكن الضرورية أخص البسائط، والوقتية أخص من المركبات الباقية، ومتى لم تنعكسا لم ينعكس شيء منها، لما عرفت أنّ انعكاس العام مستلزم لانعكاس الخاص.
[79 - Conversion of partial negatives of specific conditionals and specific 'urfiya] He said: If it is particular, then the specific conditional and specific 'urfiya convert to a specific 'urfiyya. Because if it is true necessarily — or always — 'Some J is not B as long as not always' then it is true always that 'Some B is not J as long as B is not always'. For we posit the subject's essence, which is J is D; then D is J actually and D is B also by the ruling of non-permanence, and D is not J as long as B, otherwise D would be J while it is B, and then B while it is J — but it was 'not B as long as J' — this is a contradiction. And if J and B are true of D and they are incompatible in it, then it is true that 'Some B is not J as long as B is not always' — which is what is required. As for the others, they do not convert, because it is true necessarily that 'Some animal is not human' and 'Necessarily, some moon is not eclipsed at the time of quadrature not always', yet their converse by absolute possibility, which is the most general of modalities, is false. But necessity is the most specific of the simple modalities, and the temporal is the most specific of the remaining composite ones. And when these two do not convert, none of them convert, because you have known that the conversion of the general entails the conversion of the specific.
Source pages p. 351

Commentary
This passage discusses the conversion of partial negative propositions that are specific conditionals (مشروطة خاصة) or specific 'urfiya (عرقية خاصة). The author states that such a proposition, when it is particular (i.e., starts with 'some'), converts to a specific 'urfiyya. He provides a proof using a subject-term variable: suppose the proposition 'Some J is not B as long as J is not always' is true with necessity or always. Convert it to 'Some B is not J as long as B is not always'. To prove this, we consider an individual D that is J actually. By the non-permanence condition, D is also B. If D were not J as long as B, then D would be J when it is B, which would mean B when it is J — contradicting the original that D is not B as long as J. Hence, D is J and B, and since they are incompatible (from the original), D is not J as long as B. This yields the required conversion. Next, the author notes that other types of conditionals (like necessary or temporal) do not convert. He gives examples: 'Some animal is not human' is necessarily true, and 'Some moon is not eclipsed at the time of quadrature not always' is also necessarily true, but their converses by absolute possibility (the most general modality) are false. Since necessity is the most specific of simple modalities and the temporal is the most specific of composite ones, and if these two do not convert, then none of the conditionals convert. This is because the conversion of the general (absolute possibility) would entail conversion of the specific, but since the specific fails, the general does not hold.

العرقية الخاصة
الإمكان العام
الضرورية
الوقتية

B) It converts to a specific 'urfiyya.
C) It does not convert.
D) It converts to a necessary universal.

Key Takeaway
The conversion of partial negative conditionals depends on the modality: only specific conditional and specific 'urfiyya convert to a specific 'urfiyya; necessary and temporal types do not, preventing conversion of all modal conditionals.

Conversion of Partial Negative Propositions: Conditioned and Customary
Author explains which partial negatives convert and provides proof for the conversion rule.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify which partial negative propositions convert (conditioned and special customary) and validate the conversion using the proof from the text.

Core Idea
Only two partial negatives convert, and they require a special customary modal force.

In this passage, the author explains which partial negative propositions undergo conversion (aks). Not all partial negatives convert—only the conditioned (mashruta) and special customary ('urfiyya khassa) do, and they convert to special customary. The proof uses a careful assumption to show the conversion holds. Understanding this is crucial for modal logic in Islamic intellectual tradition.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types that convert
From the passage, partial negatives that convert are the conditioned (mashruta) and special customary ('urfiyya khassa). Note the modal qualifiers: 'as long as J' and 'not always'.
2
Understand the structure of the original proposition
The original is 'Some J is not B as long as J, not always'. It is a partial negative with a conditional duration.
3
Follow the proof steps
The author assumes an individual D that is J actually, is B at some times (by non-permanence), and is not B while J. Then argues that D is not J while B, otherwise contradiction. This yields the converted proposition 'Some B is not J as long as B, not always'.
4
Check the modality conversion
Notice that the conversion preserves the 'not always' modality, resulting in a special customary proposition. The conversion has two parts: the negative part and the non-permanence part.
5
Contrast with non-converting types
The remaining partial negatives (necessary, permanent, general, temporal) do not convert. Example: 'Some animal is not human by necessity' is true but its conversion is false because all humans are animals by necessity.

Statement: You have known that universal negatives are seven; some of them do not convert, and six of them convert. As for partial negatives, only the conditioned and special customary convert; they convert to special customary. Because if it is true by necessity or always that 'not some J is B as long as J, not always', then it is true always that 'not some B is J as long as B, not always' because we assume that part — which is J and not B as long as J, not always — to be D, a particular J actually — that is clear — and D is B by virtue of the non-permanence, and D is not J as long as B, otherwise D would be J at some times when it is B, so it would be B at some times when it is J, because when two descriptions coincide on an essence, each is established at the time of the other; and D was not B as long as J — this is a contradiction. And since J and B hold of D and are mutually exclusive in it—that is, whenever J is, B is not, and whenever B is, J is not—then 'some B is not J as long as B, not always' is true. For when 'B' is true of D and 'not J as long as B' is true, then 'some B is not J as long as B' is true, which is the first part of the conversion. And since 'J' and 'B' are true of it, 'some B is J actually' is true of it, which is the non-permanence of the conversion; thus the conversion is true with its two parts together. As for the remaining partial negatives, they do not convert, because they are either the four permanent and general negatives, or the seven mentioned negatives and the more specific of the four which are necessary, and the more specific of the seven which are temporal; neither of them converts. As for the necessary, because our statement 'some animal is not human by necessity' is true while 'some human is not animal by general possibility' is false, since 'every human is animal by necessity'.
Source pages p. 351

Commentary
This passage is a discussion on the conversion (aks) of partial negative propositions in modal logic, specifically those with conditional and customary modalities. The author states that among partial negatives, only two types convert: the conditioned (mashruta) and the special customary ('urfiyya khassa). Both convert to a special customary negative. The proof proceeds by constructing an assumption. Let 'J' be the subject and 'B' the predicate. Suppose 'not some J is B as long as J, not always' is true. Then there exists an individual D that is actually J, is B at some times (by the non-permanence condition), and is not B while J. The author argues that D is not J while B, otherwise a contradiction arises because the two descriptions would coincide. Since J and B both hold of D but not simultaneously, we can assert that 'some B is not J as long as B, not always' is true, which is the converted proposition. The conversion has two parts: the first is the negative 'some B is not J as long as B', and the second is the non-permanence (i.e., 'some B is J actually') which comes from the fact that D is B and also J at other times. The passage then explains why the remaining partial negatives do not convert. These include the permanent negatives (da'imatan), general negatives ('ammatan), the necessary (daruriyya), and the temporal (waqtiyya). For example, the necessary negative 'some animal is not human by necessity' is true, but its conversion 'some human is not animal by general possibility' is false because all humans are necessarily animals. Thus, the conversion fails because the modality is not preserved. The argument uses the general principle that the conversion of a more general proposition entails the conversion of its specific forms.

Key Terms
عكس (aks): The operation of converting a proposition by swapping subject and predicate while preserving truth under certain conditions. [Note: No significant shift; this term is stable across the tradition.]
سوالب جزئية (sawālib juz'iyya): Partial negative propositions, i.e., 'Some J is not B' with a modal qualification. [Note: No significant shift.]
مشروطة (mashrūṭa): A proposition where the predicate holds under a condition, e.g., 'as long as J'. [Note: In earlier texts, mashruta may refer to a different kind of conditional; here it is specifically a modal qualifier.]
عرفية خاصة ('urfiyya khāṣṣa): A special customary proposition indicating a temporal condition 'not always'. [Note: This term is standard in later commentaries.]
لادوام (lā dawām): Lack of perpetuity; the predicate does not hold at all times. [Note: No significant shift.]
بالضرورة (bi-ḍarūra): Necessity; the proposition is necessarily true. [Note: Stable across the tradition.]

Key Takeaway

Conversion of Particular Negative Propositions
Author classifies negative propositions and proves two particular negatives convert.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify which particular negative propositions convert and reconstruct the proof of their conversion using the method of assumption.

Core Idea
Only two modal particular negatives convert: the conditioned and the special customary.

In previous lessons, you learned that among universal negatives, seven do not convert and six do. Now we turn to particular negatives, where the situation is more restrictive. The author states that only two types of particular negatives convert: the conditioned (al-mashrūṭa) and the special customary (al-ʿurfiyya al-khāṣṣa). He then provides a detailed proof to show that the converse of such a proposition is a special customary proposition. This proof uses an assumption about a particular instance and proceeds by contradiction.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two converting types
From the opening sentence, note which particular negatives the author says do convert: the conditioned (المشروطة) and the special customary (العرفية الخاصة). He claims they convert to a special customary proposition.
The two types are modal negatives with specific temporal conditions.
2
Restate the conversion claim
The claim is: If the proposition 'Not some C is B as long as C, not always' is true, then its converse 'Not some B is C as long as B, not always' is also true. Understand the modal operators: 'as long as' (dam) and 'not always' (lā dā'iman).
This is a modal immediate inference, not a simple categorical conversion.
3
Assume a witness D
The proof introduces D as the 'some C' that is not B as long as C (but not always). D is actually C, and D is B non-continuously (i.e., sometimes not B). This sets up the conditions for the argument.
The existence of such a D is guaranteed by the truth of the original proposition.
4
Derive that D is not C as long as B
Suppose contrary: D is C as long as B. Then at some time when D is B, D would be C, so B would apply at times when C applies, contradicting the assumption that D is not B as long as C. Hence D is not C as long as B.
This is a reductio ad absurdum step.
5
Establish both parts of the converse
From D being B and not C as long as B, we get 'some B is not C as long as B' (first part). Also, D is both C and B actually, so 'some B C is actual' (second part). Together they form the converse: some B is not C as long as B, not always.
The converse is a special customary proposition, as claimed.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القول : قد عرفت أنّ السوالب الكلّيّة سبع منها لا تنعكس، وست منها تنعكس؛ فالسوالب الجزئية لا تنعكس إلا المشروطة والعرفية الخاصتان، فإنهما تنعكسان عرفية خاصة.
لأنه إذا صدق بالضرورة - أو دائماً - ليس بعض (ج ب) مادام (ج) لا دائماً، صدق دائماً ليس بعض (ب ج) مادام (ب) لا دائماً. لأنا نفرض ذلك البعض - الذي هو (ج) وليس (ب) مادام (ج) لا دائماً - (د). فرد (ج) بالفعل - وهو ظاهر - و (د ب) بحكم اللادوام. و (د) ليس (ج) مادام (ب) وإلا لكان (د ج) في بعض أوقات كونه (ب) فيكون (ب) في بعض أوقات كونه (ج)، لأن الوصفين إذا تقارنا على ذات يثبت كل منهما في وقت الآخر؛ وقد كان (د) ليس (ب) مادام (ج) - هذا خلف. وإذ قد صدق (ج) و(ب) على (د) وتنافيا فيه – أي متى كان (ج) لم يكن (ب)، ومتى كان (ب) لم يكن (ج) – صدق «بعض (ب) ليس (ج) مادام (ب) لا دائماً». فإنه لما صدق على (د ب) وصدق «ليس (ج) مادام (ب)» صدق «بعض (ب) ليس (ج) مادام (ب)» وهو الجزء الأول من العكس. ولما صدق عليه أنه (ج) و (ب) صدق عليه «بعض (ب ج) بالفعل» وهو لادوام العكس. فيصدق العكس بجزأيه معاً.
Statement: You have known that among universal negatives seven do not convert and six convert; as for particular negatives, they do not convert except the conditioned and the two special customaries, for they convert as a special customary. Because if 'necessarily—or always—not some C is B as long as C, not always' is true, then 'always not some B is C as long as B, not always' is true. For we assume that part which is C and not B as long as C, not always, (call it D). So D is C actually—which is apparent—and D is B by virtue of non-continuity. And D is not C as long as B, otherwise D C would be in some times of its being B, so B would be in some times of its being C, because when two descriptions coincide upon a subject, each is established at the time of the other; and it was that D is not B as long as C—this is a contradiction. And since C and B are true of D and they are mutually exclusive in it—i.e., whenever it is C it is not B, and whenever it is B it is not C— then 'some B is not C as long as B, not always' is true. For when [D] is true of B and 'not C as long as B' is true, then 'some B is not C as long as B' is true, and that is the first part of the converse. And when it is true of it that it is C and B, then 'some B C is actual' is true, and that is the non-continuity of the converse. Thus the converse with both parts is true.
Source pages p. 352

Commentary
This passage classifies negative propositions with respect to conversion and provides a detailed proof for the conversion of two particular negatives. The author first states the known classification: among universal negatives, seven do not convert and six do; among particular negatives, only two types convert—the 'conditioned' (المشروطة) and the 'special customary' (العرفية الخاصة). The proof then shows that when a proposition like 'Not some C is B as long as C, not always' is true, its converse 'Not some B is C as long as B, not always' also holds. The proof proceeds by assuming a particular D that is C actually and B non-continuously, then showing that D is not C as long as B, and from the mutual exclusion of C and B in D, both parts of the converse are established. This is a standard immediate inference in logic, using the technique of conditional proof.

Proving Non-Conversion of Particular Negatives
Author lists particular negatives that do not convert and answers an objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify which modality-factored particular negatives do not convert and justify their non-conversion using necessary and temporal counterexamples.

Core Idea
Some particular negatives resist conversion because their converses would require a different modality that does not hold.

This passage addresses a subtle point in immediate inference: not all particular negatives convert. The author meticulously lists which ones fail and gives concrete examples. He also anticipates an objection about redundancy and defends his method.

Guided Path
1
List the non-converting types
Identify the two groups: (1) the four perpetuals and two generals, and (2) the seven mentioned plus the more specific necessary and temporal. Note that these are the 'remaining' particular negatives after the ones that do convert.
The groups are defined by modality and quantity.
2
Analyze the necessary example
The original: 'Some animal is not human necessarily' is true. The converse with general possibility: 'Some human is not animal with general possibility' is false because every human is animal necessarily. Thus the necessary particular negative does not convert.
The modality in the converse must match the original's implication, but it fails.
3
Analyze the temporal example
The original: 'Some moon is not eclipsed at the time of quadrature, not always' is true. The converse: 'Some eclipsed is not a moon with general possibility' is false because every eclipsed is moon necessarily. Thus the temporal particular negative does not convert.
Notice the shift from temporal to general possibility in the converse.
4
Follow the objection and response
Objection: Since universal negatives don't convert, their particulars also don't convert, so the detailed examples are unnecessary. Response: This is an independent argument, and specifying the method is not part of disputation protocol. The author shows a direct proof for particulars.
The objection is based on the principle that if the more specific fails, the more general fails, but the author prefers direct demonstration.
5
Apply the principle: if more specific fails, more general fails
The author uses the more specific cases (necessary and temporal) to prove that the more general groups (perpetual, general) also do not convert. Understand why this follows logically: if the specific fails, the general must fail because its conversion would entail the specific's conversion.
This is a standard logical implication used in the argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما السوالب الجزئية الباقية فلا تنعكس، لأنها إما السوالب الأربع الدائمتان والعامتان وإما السوالب السبع المذكورة وأخص الأربع التي هي الضرورية وأخص السبع الوقتية؛ وشيء منهما لا ينعكس: أما الضرورية فلصدق قولنا: «بعض الحيوان ليس بإنسان بالضرورة» مع كذب «بعض الإنسان ليس بحيوان بالإمكان العام» إذ «كل إنسان حيوان بالضرورة». وأما الوقتية فلصدق: «بعض القمر ليس بمنخسف وقت التربيع لا دائماً»، وكذب «بعض المنخسف ليس بقمر بالإمكان العام»، لأن «كل منخسف قمر بالضرورة»؛ وإذا لم ينعكس الأخص لم ينعكس الأعم، لأن انعكاس الأعم مستلزم لانعكاس الأخص. لا يقال: قد تبيّن أن السوالب السبع الكلية لا تنعكس، ويلزم من ذلك عدم انعكاس جزئياتها، لأن الكلية أخص من الجزئية، وعدم انعكاس الأخص ملزوم لعدم انعكاس الأعم فكان في ذلك كفاية، فلا حاجة إلى هذا التطويل. لانا نقول: هذا طريق آخر لبيان عدم انعكاس الجزئيات، وتعين الطريق ليس من دأب المناظرة.
As for the remaining particular negatives, they do not convert, because they are either the four perpetuals and the two generals, or the seven mentioned and the more specific of the four which are the necessary and the more specific of the seven which are the temporal; and none of them convert. As for the necessary, because our statement 'Some animal is not a human necessarily' is true while 'Some human is not an animal with general possibility' is false, since 'Every human is an animal necessarily'. As for the temporal, because 'Some moon is not eclipsed at the time of quadrature, not always' is true, and 'Some eclipsed is not a moon with general possibility' is false, because 'Every eclipsed is a moon necessarily'. And if the more specific does not convert, the more general does not convert, because the conversion of the more general entails the conversion of the more specific. It cannot be said: It has been shown that the seven universal negatives do not convert, and from that it follows that their particulars do not convert, because the universal is more specific than the particular, and the non-conversion of the more specific implies the non-conversion of the more general, so that would be sufficient, and there is no need for this elaboration. But we say: This is another way to show the non-conversion of particulars, and specifying the way is not part of the practice of disputation.

Commentary
This passage continues the discussion of conversion by listing particular negatives that do not convert. The author divides them into two groups: (1) the four perpetuals and the two generals, and (2) the seven mentioned plus the more specific of the four (necessary) and more specific of the seven (temporal). Two examples are given: a necessary particular negative ('Some animal is not a human necessarily') and a temporal particular negative ('Some moon is not eclipsed at quadrature, not always'). Both have true originals but false converses, showing non-conversion. The author then addresses an objection: since universal negatives do not convert, their particulars also do not convert by the rule that the more specific (universal) failing implies the more general (particular) failing; so why the long discussion? The reply is that this is an independent argument, and specifying the method is not part of disputation protocol.

Conversion of Affirmative Modal Propositions
Conversion rules for modal affirmatives

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the conversion rules for affirmative modal propositions to determine the correct converse for necessary, perpetual, general, and special modalities.

Core Idea
Modal affirmatives convert only to a weaker temporal form.

In this lesson, we examine the author's treatment of conversion ('aks) for affirmative propositions when modalities are considered. While simple affirmatives do not convert universally due to predicate breadth, modal affirmatives such as necessary and perpetual do convert to an absolute temporal proposition. The author also explains a special case for the two special modalities. Understanding these rules is essential for properly transforming premises in syllogistic reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the original proposition type
Determine whether the proposition is a simple affirmative (without modality) or a modal affirmative. If modal, note the specific modality: necessary, perpetual, general (common), or special (common conditioned).
The passage lists four modalities: necessary, perpetual, two general, two special.
2
Apply the rule for simple affirmatives
Recognize that no simple affirmative (universal or particular) converts universally because the predicate may be broader than the subject. Example: 'Every intoxicant is prohibited' cannot convert to 'Every prohibited is intoxicant'.
The author uses the example 'Every human is an animal' but that is a standard non-Islamic example; we substitute an Islamic example here.
3
Apply the conversion rule for general modal affirmatives
For necessary, perpetual, and the two general modal affirmatives, the converse is an absolute temporal proposition: 'Some B is C when it is B'. The proof is by contradiction: if this converse were false, then 'No B is C as long as B' would be true, leading to an absurd conclusion with the original.
The absolute temporal (hiniyya mutlaqa) is weaker than the original modality.
4
Apply the conversion rule for special modal affirmatives
The two special modalities convert to an absolute temporal proposition restricted by non-continuity (lādawām), i.e., 'Some B is C when it is B, but not always'. This is because the original premise itself has a 'not always' condition.
The passage ends abruptly, but the rule is inferred from the available text.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال : وأما الموجبة - كلّيّة كانت أو جزئية - فلا تنعكس كلية أصلا، لاحتمال كون المحمول أعم من الموضوع، كقولنا: «كل إنسان حيوان». وأما في الجهة: فالضرورية والدائمة والعامّتان تنعكس حينية مطلقة، لأنه إذا صدق «كل (ج) (ب) - بإحدى الجهات الأربع المذكورة – فـ«بعض (ب ج) حين هو (ب)» وإلا فـ «لا شيء من (ب) (ج) مادام (ب)» وهو مع الأصل ينتج لا شيء من (ج ج) دائماً في الضرورية والدائمة، ومادام (ج) في العامتين - وهو محال. وأما الخاصتان فتنعكسان حينيّة مطلقة مقيدة باللادوام. أما الحينية المطلقة فلكونها لازمة لعامتيهما، وأما قيد اللادوام في الأصل
He said: As for the affirmative—whether universal or particular—it does not convert as universal at all, due to the possibility that the predicate is more general than the subject, like our statement 'Every human is an animal'. As for modality: the necessary, perpetual, and the two general convert to absolute temporal, because if 'Every C is B' is true with one of the four mentioned modalities, then 'Some B is C when it is B'; otherwise, 'Nothing of B is C as long as B', and that with the original yields 'Nothing of C C is C always' in the necessary and perpetual, and 'as long as C' in the two generals—which is impossible. As for the two specials, they convert to absolute temporal restricted by non-continuity. As for the absolute temporal, it is because it is entailed by their general; and as for the restriction of non-continuity in the original
Source pages p. 353

Commentary
This passage discusses conversion of affirmative propositions. The author begins by stating that no affirmative—universal or particular—converts universally (i.e., as a universal affirmative) because the predicate may be broader than the subject, as in 'Every human is an animal' (which does not convert to 'Every animal is a human'). However, when modalities are introduced, some affirmatives do convert to a weaker form. The necessary, perpetual, and the two general modal affirmatives convert to an absolute temporal proposition (hiniyya mutlaqa). The proof is by contradiction: if the converse 'Some B is C when it is B' is false, then 'No B is C as long as B' would be true; combining that with the original yields an absurdity. The two special customary/conditioned affirmatives convert to an absolute temporal proposition restricted by non-continuity (i.e., with a 'not always' qualifier). The explanation of the restriction is cut off but suggests it comes from the non-continuity in the original premise.

الحينية المطلقة
اللادوام

Key Takeaway
Simple affirmatives do not convert universally, but necessary, perpetual, and general modal affirmatives convert to an absolute temporal proposition, while special modalities convert to an absolute temporal restricted by non-continuity.

Conversion of Temporal Negatives and Dialectical Response
Author discusses conversion of temporal negatives and rebuts an objection of redundancy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why temporal negative propositions fail to convert and respond to the objection of redundancy using the principle of specific and general.

Core Idea
Temporal negatives don't convert; the specific-general principle blocks them.

In this passage, the author explores conversion of temporal negative propositions using an example from lunar eclipses. He demonstrates that a particular negative can be true while its converse is false, thereby establishing a general rule: if the more specific (universal) does not convert, the more general (particular) cannot. He then addresses a potential objection that this discussion is redundant, clarifying that multiple valid approaches are permissible in dialectic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the temporal negative example
Look at the first part: 'Some moon is not eclipsed at the time of the square' – what does this proposition mean in terms of time? It means there is a time when it's not eclipsed, but it doesn't deny the possibility.
2
Test conversion by swapping subject and predicate
Convert 'Some S is not P' to 'Some P is not S' for a temporal negative. Check if the converse is true. The author says it's false because every eclipsed thing is necessarily a moon.
3
Apply the specific-general principle
Notice the author uses the relationship between the universal (more specific) and particular (more general). Since the universal negative does not convert, the particular negative does not convert either. This is because if the more general (particular) did convert, it would entail the more specific (universal) converting, which is false.
4
Analyze the dialectical objection and response
The objection says this proof is unnecessary because we already know universals don't convert. The response says this is just another valid method; dialectic does not require a single method.
The author shows that multiple proofs can coexist without redundancy.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الوقتية فلصدق : بعض القمر ليس بمنخسف وقت التربيع لا دائماً، وكذب بعض المنخسف ليس بقمر بالإمكان العام، لأن كل منخسف قمر بالضرورة؛ وإذا لم ينعكس الأخص لم ينعكس الأعم، لأن انعكاس الأعم مستلزم لانعكاس الأخص. لا يقال : قد تبيّن أن السوالب السبع الكلية لا تنعكس، ويلزم من ذلك عدم انعكاس جزئياتها، لأن الكلية أخص من الجزئية، وعدم انعكاس الأخص ملزوم لعدم انعكاس الأعم فكان في ذلك كفاية، فلا حاجة إلى هذا التطويل. لانا نقول : هذا طريق آخر لبيان عدم انعكاس الجزئيات، وتعين الطريق ليس من دأب المناظرة.
As for the temporal [negative] proposition, it is because it is true to say 'Some moon is not eclipsed at the time of the square, not always,' and false to say 'Some eclipsed thing is not a moon with universal possibility,' because every eclipsed thing is necessarily a moon. And if the more specific does not convert, the more general does not convert, because the conversion of the more general entails the conversion of the more specific. It should not be said: 'It has been made clear that the seven universal negatives do not convert, and from that follows the non-conversion of their particulars, because the universal is more specific than the particular, and non-conversion of the more specific entails non-conversion of the more general, so that would be sufficient, so there is no need for this lengthy exposition.' For we say: 'This is another way to show the non-conversion of particulars, and the determination of the method is not the practice of dialectic.'
Source pages p. 353

Commentary
This passage is an exposition on the conversion of temporal negative propositions (الوقتية) within the broader topic of simple conversion (العكس المستوي). The author begins by giving a concrete example from astronomy: the proposition 'Some moon is not eclipsed at the time of the square (i.e., quadrature) at all times' is true, but its converse 'Some eclipsed thing is not a moon with universal possibility' is false, because every eclipsed thing is necessarily a moon. This shows that the temporal negative does not convert. The author then explains the logical principle: if the more specific (الأخص, here the universal negative) does not convert, the more general (الأعم, the particular negative) also does not convert, because conversion of the more general would imply conversion of the more specific. A dialectical objection is raised: since the seven universal negatives are already known not to convert, and the non-conversion of particulars follows from that by the same principle, the detailed discussion of temporal negatives is redundant. The author responds that this is simply another valid method of proof, and determining which method to use is not the concern of dialectic (المناظرة), which accepts multiple valid paths. The passage thus reinforces the rule that no negative proposition converts universally.

الأخص

C) Particulars always convert.
D) Universals always convert.

Key Takeaway
Temporal negatives do not convert, and the principle of specific and general shows that non-conversion of universals implies non-conversion of particulars.

Why Affirmative Propositions Never Convert Universally
Author introduces a fundamental limitation on conversion of affirmatives.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify why an affirmative universal proposition cannot convert to a universal converse, and test this rule with examples from Islamic sciences.

Core Idea
Affirmative propositions do not convert universally because the predicate may be broader than the subject.

When we say 'Every human is an animal,' it seems natural to think that 'Every animal is a human' would also be true. But it's not. This lesson explains the logical rule that prevents universal conversion of affirmative propositions, a foundational tool for evaluating arguments in Islamic theology and jurisprudence.

Guided Path
1
State the rule
The passage states that no affirmative, universal or particular, converts universally. That is, from 'Every J is B' we cannot conclude 'Every B is J'.
This is a general rule, not an exception.
2
Identify the reason
The reason is that the predicate (B) may be more general than the subject (J). For example, 'human' is a subset of 'animal'.
The converse would be false if the predicate includes things outside the subject.
3
Test with an example from Kalam
Consider 'Every created thing is temporally originated'. Does it convert universally? 'Every temporally originated thing is created' might be false because attributes like knowledge are temporally originated but not created in the same sense.
This shows the rule applies in theological reasoning.
4
Generalize the insight
This rule applies to all affirmative propositions regardless of modality. The only exception is when subject and predicate are coextensive, but that is not guaranteed.
The passage uses this as a preliminary before discussing modal conversions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال : وأما الموجبة - كلّيّة كانت أو جزئية - فلا تنعكس كلية أصلا، لاحتمال كون المحمول أعم من الموضوع، كقولنا: «كل إنسان حيوان». وأما في الجهة: فالضرورية والدائمة والعامّتان تنعكس حينية مطلقة، لأنه إذا صدق «كل (ج) (ب) - بإحدى الجهات الأربع المذكورة – فـ«بعض (ب ج) حين هو (ب)» وإلا فـ « لا شيء من (ب) (ج) مادام (ب)» وهو مع الأصل ينتج لا شيء من (ج ج) دائماً في الضرورية والدائمة، ومادام (ج) في العامتين - وهو محال. وأما الخاصتان فتنعكسان حينيّة مطلقة مقيدة باللادوام أما الحينية المطلقة فلكونها لازمة لعامتيهما، وأما قيد اللادوام في الأصل الكلي فلاله لو كذب «بعض (ب) ليس (ج) بالفعل» لصدق «كل ب ج دائماً» فنضمّه إلى الجزء الأوّل من الأصل - وهو قولنا: «بالضرورة - أو دائماً - كل(ج) (ب) مادام (ج)» ينتج كل ب ب دائماً ونضمه إلى الجزء الثاني أيضاً، وهو قولنا: لاشيء من (ج) (ب) بالإطلاق العام» ينتج «لاشيء من (ب) (ب) بالإطلاق العام فيلزم اجتماع النقيضين وهو محال. وأما في الجزئي: فيفرض الموضوع (د) فهو ليس (ج) بالفعل، وإلا لكان (ج) دائماً فـ(ب) دائماً لدوام الباء بدوام الجيم، لكن اللازم باطل لنفيـه الأصل باللادوام.
He said: As for the affirmative proposition, whether universal or particular, it does not convert universally at all, because it is possible that the predicate is more general than the subject, as in our saying 'Every human is an animal.' As for modality: Necessary, perpetual, and the two common [affirmatives] convert to an absolute temporal [particular] because if 'Every J is B' with one of the four mentioned modalities is true, then 'Some B is J while it is B' [is true]; otherwise, 'No B is J as long as it is B' which together with the original produces 'No J is J always' in necessary and perpetual, and 'as long as J' in the two common [modalities] — which is absurd. As for the two special [affirmatives], they convert to an absolute temporal [particular] restricted by non-permanence. As for the absolute temporal, because it is necessary for their two common [modalities]; and as for the restriction of non-permanence in the original universal, because if 'Some B is not J actually' is false, then 'Every B is J always' is true, so we combine it with the first part of the original — which is our saying: 'necessarily or always every J is B as long as J' — it produces 'Every B is B always'; and we combine it also with the second part, which is our saying: 'No J is B with absolute universality' — it produces 'No B is B with absolute universality', thus contradictories combine, which is absurd. As for the particular: the subject (D) is assumed; it is not J actually, otherwise it would be J always, so B always because of the permanence of B with permanence of J, but the consequent is false because the original denies it by non-permanence.
Source pages p. 353

Commentary
This passage is a detailed exposition on the conversion of affirmative propositions (الموجبات) considering modal qualifiers. The author first states the fundamental rule: no affirmative proposition, whether universal or particular, converts universally in quantity (i.e., as a universal). The reason is that the predicate may be more general than the subject, as in 'Every human is an animal' – its universal converse 'Every animal is a human' is false. The author then turns to modal propositions (الجهة). He identifies four relevant modalities: necessary (الضرورية), perpetual (الدائمة), and two common (العامتان) – likely 'as long as J' and 'as long as B' – and two special (الخاصتان) – possibly 'actual' or 'non-permanent'. For the first four, he provides a proof by contradiction: assume the converse is false, then the contradictory 'No B is J as long as B' holds, which combined with the original 'Every J is B' yields 'No J is J always' or 'as long as J', which is absurd. Therefore the converse 'Some B is J while it is B' (an absolute temporal particular) is true. For the two special modalities, the converse is an absolute temporal particular restricted by 'non-permanence' (اللادوام). The argument uses a different reduction: if the converse were false, that leads to 'Every B is J always', which combined with the original yields an absurdity. For the particular case (الجزئي), the subject is assumed as individual D, and a similar reduction shows the converse holds conditionally. The passage is a classic example of using proof by contradiction (الخلف) to establish conversion rules in modal logic.

الموجبة
عكس كلية

Key Takeaway
Affirmative propositions cannot be converted universally because the predicate may be broader than the subject.

Modal Affirmative Conversions: Necessary, Perpetual, and Common
Author explains modal conversion rules with proofs by contradiction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the correct converse of a modal affirmative proposition and verify it using the proof by contradiction method.

Core Idea
Necessary, perpetual, and common modal affirmatives convert to an absolute temporal particular proposition.

In the previous lesson, we saw that affirmatives do not convert universally. But in modal logic, there are specific rules when modality is added. This lesson unpacks how propositions with modalities like 'necessarily' or 'always' convert to a weaker form: 'some B is J at some time when it is B'.

Guided Path
1
Identify the modalities covered
The author lists necessary (الضرورية), perpetual (الدائمة), and two common (العامتان). Understand that necessity implies always true, perpetual implies always in actuality, common may be 'as long as it is J'.
These four are grouped together for conversion.
2
State the conversion rule
These convert to an absolute temporal particular (الحينية المطلقة): 'Some B is J while it is B'.
The converse is particular in quantity and temporal in modality.
3
Understand the proof by contradiction
Assume the converse is false, i.e., 'No B is J as long as B'. Combine with the original 'Every J is B' to derive 'No J is J always' or 'as long as J', which is absurd.
This proves the converse must be true.
4
Apply to an Islamic example
Consider 'Every prophet is infallible necessarily'. The converse is 'Some infallible being is a prophet at some time when the infallible being exists'.
This shows the rule applies in theological discussions about prophethood.
5
Note the exception for special modalities
The two special modalities convert to an absolute temporal particular restricted by non-permanence (اللادوام). That means the converse holds only at some times, not always.
The proof for special modalities uses a different reduction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

He said: As for the affirmative proposition, whether universal or particular, it does not convert universally at all, because it is possible that the predicate is more general than the subject, as in our saying 'Every human is an animal.' As for modality: Necessary, perpetual, and the two common [affirmatives] convert to an absolute temporal [particular] because if 'Every J is B' with one of the four mentioned modalities is true, then 'Some B is J while it is B' [is true]; otherwise, 'No B is J as long as it is B' which together with the original produces 'No J is J always' in necessary and perpetual, and 'as long as J' in the two common [modalities] — which is absurd. As for the two special [affirmatives], they convert to an absolute temporal [particular] restricted by non-permanence. As for the absolute temporal, because it is necessary for their two common [modalities]; and as for the restriction of non-permanence in the original universal, because if 'Some B is not J actually' is false, then 'Every B is J always' is true, so we combine it with the first part of the original — which is our saying: 'necessarily or always every J is B as long as J' — it produces 'Every B is B always'; and we combine it also with the second part, which is our saying: 'No J is B with absolute universality' — it produces 'No B is B with absolute universality', thus contradictories combine, which is absurd. As for the particular: the subject (D) is assumed; it is not J actually, otherwise it would be J always, so B always because of the permanence of B with permanence of J, but the consequent is false because the original denies it by non-permanence.

Commentary
This passage is a detailed exposition on the conversion of affirmative propositions (الموجبات) considering modal qualifiers. The author first states the fundamental rule: no affirmative proposition, whether universal or particular, converts universally in quantity (i.e., as a universal). The reason is that the predicate may be more general than the subject, as in 'Every human is an animal' – its universal converse 'Every animal is a human' is false. For the particular case (الجزئي), the subject is assumed as individual D, and a similar reduction shows the converse holds conditionally. Conversion of Temporal, Existential, and Absolute Affirmatives
Author gives conversion rules for remaining modal affirmatives and summarises the general rule.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply conversion rules for temporal, existential, and absolute affirmatives and justify why affirmatives do not convert as universals.

Core Idea
Affirmatives only convert as particulars because the predicate is often broader than the subject.

In this lesson, we complete our study of conversion (al-aks) by examining the remaining modal affirmatives: temporal, existential, and absolute universal. The author shows they all convert to an absolute universal particular (some B is J), using a contradiction proof. He then summarises that all affirmatives—whether universal or particular—do not convert as universals, because the predicate can be more general. Understanding this rule is crucial for correct inference in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the propositions
Recognise the three types: temporal (e.g., 'Every J is B at some time'), existential (e.g., 'Some J is B exists'), and absolute universal (e.g., 'Every J is B' without modality). They are all affirmative modal propositions.
These differ from necessary and perpetual affirmatives covered earlier.
2
Follow the contradiction proof
Assume the converse 'Some B is J' is false. Then the universal negative 'No B is J always' is true. Combine this with the original 'Every J is B' to produce 'No J is J always', which is absurd. Therefore the converse must be true as a particular.
The proof shows conversion to an absolute universal particular, regardless of the original modality.
3
Understand the general rule for affirmatives
Affirmatives do not convert as universals. For example, 'Every human is an animal' is true, but 'Every animal is a human' is false. This holds for both universal and particular affirmatives.
The rule prevents fallacious conversion; always convert affirmatives as particulars.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الوقتيتان والوجوديتان والمطلقة العامة: فتنعكس مطلقة عامة، لأنه إذا صدق «كل (ج) (ب) - بإحدى الجهات الخمس المذكورة – فـ«بعض (ب ج) بالإطلاق العام» وإلا لصدق «لاشيء من (ب ج) دائماً» وهو مع الأصل ينتج «لاشيء من (ج ج) دائماً» وهو محال. القول : مامر كان حكم السوالب. وأما الموجبات فهي لا تنعكس في الكم كليّة، سواء كانت كلية أو جزئية، لجواز أن يكون المحمول فيها أعم من الموضوع، وامتناع حمل الخاص على كل أفراد العام ، كقولنا : «كلّ إنسان حيوان» وعكسه كلياً كاذب.

Commentary
This passage concludes the discussion on conversion by addressing the remaining affirmative modal propositions: the two temporal (الوقتيتان), the two existential (الوجوديتان), and the absolute universal (المطلقة العامة). The author states that these all convert to an absolute universal particular (مطلقة عامة), meaning 'Some B is J' without any temporal restriction. The proof is again by contradiction: if the converse were false, then 'No B is J always' would be true, which combined with the original 'Every J is B' yields 'No J is J always' — an impossibility. The author then gives a summary (القول): the preceding discussion has covered the rule for negatives. For affirmatives, the fundamental rule is that they do not convert universally in quantity (i.e., as a universal proposition) because the predicate is often more general, as in 'Every human is an animal' whose universal converse 'Every animal is a human' is false. This serves as a recap of the main principles. The passage thus ties together the entire section on conversion of categorical propositions.

Conversion of Particular Negative Propositions
Author proves conversion of particular negative via reductio.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the reductio proof for the conversion of a particular negative proposition and apply the conversion rule to Islamic science examples.

Core Idea
Particular negatives convert, proven by assuming the opposite and deriving a contradiction from the original premise.

This passage demonstrates that the proposition 'Some B is not J' (جزئية سالبة) can be converted to 'Some J is not B'. The proof uses reductio ad absurdum, leveraging the concepts of actual existence (بالفعل) and perpetuity (دوام). By following the steps, you will see how a contradiction forces the acceptance of the conversion, a tool essential for manipulating categorical propositions in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
State the conversion rule
The rule: a particular negative proposition (بعض ب ليس ج) converts to another particular negative (بعض ج ليس ب). This immediate inference is the subject of the proof.
2
Identify the original premise
The original proposition is 'Some B is not J in actuality'. The term 'بالفعل' emphasizes actual, not perpetual, existence.
3
Set up the reductio assumption
Assume for contradiction that the conversion is false, i.e., no J is not B, meaning all J are B. Then there exists an individual (د) that is B but not J actually (from the original premise).
The author uses 'د' as the assumed subject.
4
Derive the contradiction
If (د) were J always (perpetually), then because B is perpetual with J (since all J are B), (د) would be B always. But the original premise states that (د) is not J actually, contradicting the idea that it could be J always. Hence the assumption is false, and the conversion holds.
The phrase 'لدوام الباء بدوام الجيم' expresses the perpetual connection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما في الجزئي: فيفرض الموضوع (د) فهو ليس (ج) بالفعل، وإلا لكان (ج) دائماً فـ(ب) دائماً لدوام الباء بدوام الجيم، لكن اللازم باطل لنفيـه الأصل باللادوام
As for the particular [negative]: a subject (d) is assumed such that it is not actually J; otherwise it would be always J, so [it would be] always B because of the perpetuity of B with the perpetuity of J, but the necessary consequence is false because the original [premise] negates it by non-perpetuity.
Source pages p. 354

Commentary
This passage explains the conversion of a particular negative proposition. The author states that for a particular negative, we assume a subject (d) which is not actually J. If it were always J, then since the original premise says B is always J? Wait, careful: The original is likely a particular negative 'Some B is not J'. The proof: assume a subject d that is not J actually; if it were always J, then B would be always B? Hmm. I need to be careful. The commentary should follow the author's order. The passage is short and dense. I will explain: The author is proving that a particular negative proposition converts to another particular negative. He assumes a counterexample subject (د) that would violate the conversion if it existed. The argument shows that assuming such a subject leads to a contradiction with the original premise. So the conversion holds. Since the text is brief, the commentary should stick to explaining the steps as given.

Key Takeaway
The conversion of a particular negative proposition is proven by reductio: assuming its falsity leads to a contradiction with the original premise, establishing its validity.

Conversion of Temporal, Existential, and Absolute Propositions
Author states conversion rule for temporal/existential/absolute propositions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify which proposition types convert to a general absolute and reconstruct the reductio proof of this rule.

Core Idea
Temporal, existential, and absolute propositions all convert to a general absolute.

In this passage, the author presents a key rule of immediate inference: when a premise is temporally, existentially, or absolutely affirmative, its converse is a general absolute, not a modal proposition. He provides a reductio proof that demonstrates why this must be so. Understanding this rule sharpens your ability to manipulate propositions in Islamic logical arguments, especially those involving time or existence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the proposition types
The passage mentions three types: temporal (true for a specific time), existential (true as long as the subject exists), and general absolute (true without any modal condition). Note that each is characterized by its modal or temporal qualification.
Temporal and existential are often grouped together as 'modalized' in later texts.
2
State the conversion rule
The author asserts that all three types convert to a general absolute proposition. That is, the converse loses the temporal or existential qualification and becomes a simple absolute.
The converse is a particular affirmative (some B is J) with absolute mode.
3
Examine the reductio proof
Assume the converse is false, meaning 'No B is J eternally' is true (a universal negative eternal). Combine this with the original premise 'Every J is B' (with its mode). From these, a syllogism would yield 'No J is J eternally', which is impossible (a contradiction). Therefore the assumption is false, so the converse must be true.
The proof relies on the absurdity of a self-negation.
4
Apply the rule to each type
Test the rule: for a temporal proposition 'Every human is walking while it is day,' the converse is 'Some walking is human absolutely.' Do the same for existential and absolute examples.
The mode is dropped in the converse, not preserved.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الوقتيتان والوجوديتان والمطلقة العامة: فتنعكس مطلقة عامة، لأنه إذا «صدق كلّ (ج) (ب) - بإحدى الجهات الخمس المذكورة – فـ«بعض (ب) (ج) بالإطلاق العام» وإلا لصدق «لاشيء من (ب) (ج) دائماً» وهو مع الأصل ينتج «لاشيء من (ج) (ج) دائماً» وهو محال.
As for the temporal, existential, and general absolute [propositions]: they convert to a general absolute, because if 'Every J is B' is true—in one of the five modes mentioned—then 'Some B is J absolutely general' is true; otherwise 'No B is J eternally' would be true, and it together with the original yields 'No J is J eternally', which is absurd.
Source pages p. 354

Commentary
This passage describes the conversion rule for propositions that are qualified by time (temporal), existence (existential), or general absolute (unqualified). The author states that these three types all convert to a general absolute proposition. He gives a proof by reductio: assume the converse is false, i.e., 'No B is J eternally' is true. Combine that with the original premise, and you get a contradiction: 'No J is J eternally', which is impossible. Therefore, the converse must be true as an absolute particular affirmative. This shows that such modal or absolutes convert to an absolute, not to a modal.

Key Terms

وجوديتان
مطلقة عامة

Conversion of Affirmative Propositions
Author explains that affirmative propositions do not convert in quantity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to correctly determine the valid converse of any universal or particular affirmative proposition.

Core Idea
Affirmatives convert only to particular — quantity is lost.

In the previous lesson, we learned the rules for converting negative propositions. Now we turn to affirmatives. Unlike negatives, affirmative propositions cannot retain their quantity when converted. Understanding this limitation is crucial for valid immediate inference in Islamic logical reasoning, especially when evaluating arguments in fiqh and kalam.

Guided Path
1
Identify the affirmative type
Determine whether the proposition is universal affirmative (A: Every S is P) or particular affirmative (I: Some S is P).
2
Check predicate generality
Note that in many true affirmatives, the predicate is more general than the subject (e.g., 'human' is less general than 'animal'). This prevents universal conversion.
3
Apply the conversion rule
For A propositions, the valid converse is I (some P is S). For I propositions, the valid converse is also I (some P is S). Never convert an affirmative to a universal.
The converse of an A is I, not A.
4
Verify with an Islamic example
Take a true fiqh proposition: 'Every intoxicant is forbidden' (A). Its converse 'Some forbidden thing is an intoxicant' (I) is true, but 'Every forbidden thing is an intoxicant' is false.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القول : ما مر كان حكم السوالب. وأما الموجبات فهي لا تنعكس في الكم كليّة، سواء كانت كلية أو جزئية، لجواز أن يكون المحمول فيها أعم من الموضوع، وامتناع حمل الخاص على كل أفراد العام ، كقولنا : «كلّ إنسان حيوان» وعكسه كلياً كاذب.
The statement: What has passed was the rule for negatives. As for affirmatives, they do not convert in quantity at all, whether they are universal or particular, because it is possible that the predicate is more general than the subject, and it is impossible to predicate the specific of all individuals of the general, like our saying 'Every human is an animal', and its universal converse is false.
Source pages p. 354

Commentary
This passage concludes the discussion on negative conversion and introduces the rule for affirmative propositions. The author states that affirmatives, whether universal or particular, cannot convert with the same quantity. That is, a universal affirmative 'Every J is B' does not convert to a universal affirmative 'Every B is J'; it only converts to a particular affirmative 'Some B is J'. Similarly, a particular affirmative 'Some J is B' converts to a particular affirmative 'Some B is J', but not to a universal. The reason given: the predicate is usually more general than the subject. For example, 'Every human is an animal' is true, but 'Every animal is a human' is false. Therefore, affirmative conversion is limited to a particular converse.

Key Terms
موجبات
سوالب
أعم

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) Some guided person is pious
C) No pious person is guided
D) Some pious person is not guided

Key Takeaway
Affirmative propositions cannot convert with the same quantity; they always become particular affirmatives regardless of being universal or particular originally.

Conversion of Modal Propositions to Absolute Particular
Author proves that necessary, perpetual, and two general modals convert to absolute particular.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the conversion of modal propositions (necessary, perpetual, general) to absolute particular by reductio.

Core Idea
Modal propositions convert not to a modal but to a conditional absolute particular.

In this passage, the author explains how necessary, perpetual, and the two general modal propositions convert. Instead of yielding another modal, the converse is an absolute particular with the condition 'while it is B'. The proof uses reductio ad absurdum, which we will walk through step by step.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four modal types
The passage names four modal propositions: necessary (ضرورية), perpetual (دائمة), and the two general (عامتان). These are the types that convert.
The two general are defined elsewhere as 'as long as the subject exists' and 'as long as the subject is J'.
2
Understand the conversion result
The author states that each of these converts to an absolute particular, which is a particular proposition with the added condition 'while it is B' (حينية مطلقة). That is, the converse is not itself modal but conditional.
3
Reconstruct the reductio proof
Assume the converse is false: 'No B is J while it is B'. Combine with the original premise (e.g., 'Every J is B necessarily') using syllogism to derive 'No J is J necessarily/perpetually etc.', which is absurd. Therefore the converse must be true.
The proof requires the original to be affirmative.
4
Analyze the objection and response
An objector might say self-negation is possible when J does not exist. But the author replies that the original is affirmative, so J exists, making self-negation impossible. This defends the proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما في الجهة : فالضروريّة والدائمة والعامّتان تنعكس حينية مطلقة بالخلف، فإنه إذا صدق كلٌ (ج) (ب) أو «بعضه (ب)- بإحدى الجهات الأربع، أي بالضرورة أو دائماً أو مادام (ج)- وجب أن يصدق «بعض (ب ج) حين هو (ب) » ؛ وإلا لصدق نقيضه، وهو «لاشيء من (ب ج) مادام (ب)» وهو مع الأصل ينتج لا شيء من (ج ج) بالضرورة – أو دائماً» إن كان الأصل ضرورياً أو دائماً ، أو «مادام (ج)» إن كان إحدى العامتين – وهو محال. وليس لأحد أن يمنع استحالته بناء على جواز سلب الشيء عن نفسه عند عدمه، لأن الأصل موجب، فيكون (ج) موجوداً.
As for modality: the necessary, perpetual, and the two general [propositions] convert to an absolute particular by reductio, for if 'Every J is B' or 'Some J is B' is true—in one of the four modes, i.e., necessarily or perpetually or as long as J—then it is necessary that 'Some B is J while it is B' is true; otherwise its contradictory, 'No B is J as long as B' would be true, and together with the original it yields 'No J is J necessarily' or 'perpetually' if the original was necessary or perpetual, or 'as long as J' if it was one of the two general [modals]—and this is absurd. And it is not for anyone to deny its impossibility based on the permissibility of negating a thing from itself when it is non-existent, because the original is affirmative, so J exists.
Source pages p. 354

Commentary
This passage addresses the conversion of modal propositions: necessary (ضرورية), perpetual (دائمة), and the two general (عامتان) which are 'while the subject exists' and 'as long as the subject is J'? Actually, the two general are defined elsewhere as propositions that are always true as long as the subject exists or as long as the subject is described by something. The author states that these four types convert to an absolute particular (حينية مطلقة), which is a particular proposition with the condition 'while it is B', i.e., 'Some B is J while it is B'. The proof is by reductio: assume the converse is false, then its contradictory 'No B is J as long as B' is true. Combining this with the original premise leads to a contradiction: 'No J is J' with the appropriate modality, which is absurd. The author then rejects a potential objection: one might argue that self-negation is possible when the subject does not exist, but since the original premise is affirmative, J must exist, so the contradiction stands. This demonstrates that the conversion is valid.

Key Terms
ضرورية
دائمة
عامتان
حينية مطلقة

Conversion of the Two Special Modal Propositions
Author proves conversion rule for special modals with reductio.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two special modal propositions in Arabic logical notation and state their correct conversion to a temporal absolute not perpetual.

Core Idea
Special modals convert to a temporal absolute, not perpetual.

This passage deals with converting special modal propositions—those that are necessary only as long as the subject's condition holds—into a simpler form. The author proves that the converse is true at the time but not always, using a reductio argument. Understanding this conversion is crucial for handling modal syllogisms in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two special modals
Find the propositions labeled 'خاصتان' (the two special ones). They are of the form 'Every J is B necessarily or perpetually as long as J but not perpetually' or 'Some J is B as long as J but not perpetually'. Note the dual condition: the necessity holds only during the duration of J, and it is not eternal.
The Arabic uses 'بالضرورة - أو دائماً' and 'مادام (ج) لا دائماً'.
2
State the conversion rule
The author claims these convert to 'حينية مطلقة لادائمة' (temporal absolute not perpetual). That is, 'Some B is J while it is B, not perpetually'. The conversion yields a particular affirmative proposition that is true at the moment of the subject's existence but not always.
The result is not a perpetual proposition but a temporal one.
3
Follow the reductio proof for the universal case
Assume the original universal special modal is true. The proof shows that if the non-perpetuity part of the converse were false, then 'Every B is J perpetually' would hold. Combine that with the original's first part to deduce 'Every B is B perpetually' and also 'No B is B absolutely general'—a contradiction. Therefore the converse must be true.
The proof uses two syllogisms: one deriving 'Every B is B perpetually' and another deriving 'No B is B absolutely general'.
4
Note the limitation to universal originals
The author explicitly states that this demonstration does not work if the original is particular (جزئياً). For particular special modals, the conversion rule may not hold by this reasoning.
This highlights the importance of quantity in logical conversions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الخاصتان فتنعكسان حينيّة مطلقة لادائمة، فإنه إذا صدق بالضرورة - أو دائماً - كلّ (ج) (ب) - أو بعضه (ب)- مادام (ج) لا دائماً » صدق بعض (ب ج) حين هو (ب) لادائماً». أما الحينية المطلقة وهي بعض (ب) ج) حين هو (ب) فلكونها لازمة لعامتيهما. وأما اللادوام - وهو «بعض (ب) ليس (ج) بالإطلاق العام» فلانه لو كذب لصدق كل (ب) (ج) دائماً» ونضمه إلى الجزء الأول من الأصل هكذا كلّ (ب) (ج) دائماً، وبالضرورة أو دائماً كـل (ج ب) مادام (ج)» لينتج كل (ب) (ب) دائماً» ونضمه إلى الجزء الثاني – الذي هو اللادوام – ونقول : «كل (ب) (ج) دائماً ، ولاشيء من (ج ب) بالإطلاق العام» لينتج : لاشيء من (ب) (ب) بالإطلاق» فلو صدق «كل (ب ج) دائماً لزم صدق کل (ب) (ب) دائماً»، ولاشيء من (ب ب) بالإطلاق، وإنه اجتماع النقيضين، وهو محال. هذا إذا كان الأصل كليّاً، وأما إذا كان جزئياً، فلايتم فيه هذا البيان.
As for the two special [modals], they convert to a temporal absolute not perpetual, for if it is true necessarily or perpetually that 'Every J is B' or that 'Some J is B' as long as J but not perpetually, then 'Some B is J while it is B not perpetually' is true. As for the temporal absolute, i.e., 'Some B is J while it is B', it is necessary because it follows from the two general [premises]. And as for the non-perpetuity, i.e., 'Some B is not J absolutely general' — if it were false, then 'Every B is J perpetually' would be true, and we conjoin it with the first part of the original thus: 'Every B is J perpetually', and 'necessarily or perpetually every J is B as long as J' to produce 'Every B is B perpetually'; and we conjoin it with the second part—which is the non-perpetuity—and say: 'Every B is J perpetually, and no J is B absolutely general' to produce 'No B is B absolutely general'. So if 'Every B is J perpetually' were true, then both 'Every B is B perpetually' and 'No B is B absolutely general' would be true, which is a contradiction and impossible. This is if the original is universal; if it is particular, this demonstration does not hold.
Source pages p. 355

Commentary
This passage explains the conversion of the two special modal propositions (خاصتان), which are propositions that are necessary or perpetual but only 'as long as J' and not perpetually. They convert to a 'temporal absolute not perpetual' (حينية مطلقة لادائمة), which means a particular proposition that is true at the time the subject is B but not always. The proof is again by reductio, but more complex because the original has two parts: one part is the modal condition (as long as J), and the other is the non-perpetuity (not always). The author shows that if the non-perpetuity is false, then 'Every B is J perpetually' must be true, and combining that with the original's first part yields a contradiction. The proof only works for universal originals; for particular originals, the reasoning fails.

Key Terms
خاصتان
لادائمة
اللادوام
حينية مطلقة

Key Terms from Source Passage

D) No valid conversion

Key Takeaway
The two special modal propositions convert to a temporal absolute not perpetual, and the proof by contradiction only works for universal originals.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Conversion of Universal Propositions and the Reductio Proof
Author proves conversion of universal propositions using reductio and rebuts objection

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the reductio proof for the simple conversion of universal negative propositions and explain why the objection about subject non-existence fails.

Core Idea
The impossibility of a thing being not itself guarantees the conversion of a universal negative.

In the Islamic logical tradition, the simple conversion of universal negative propositions (E) is a cornerstone of immediate inference. This lesson walks through the classical reductio proof and addresses a subtle objection about subject existence, clarifying the conditions under which conversion is valid.

Guided Path
1
Identify the proposition being converted
The passage deals with a universal proposition (al-ʿāmmatān) — likely a universal negative. The conversion swaps subject and predicate while preserving quality and truth.
For universal negative 'No A is B', the simple conversion yields 'No B is A'.
2
Understand the reductio method
The proof assumes the contradictory of the converted proposition (i.e., that the conversion is false) and combines it with the original to derive an impossibility.
This is known as al-khalf (reductio ad absurdum).
3
Trace the derivation of impossibility
From the assumption 'Some B is A' (contradictory of 'No B is A') and the original 'No A is B', a syllogism yields 'Nothing from A is A', which is impossible because a thing cannot be negated from itself.
The formal derivation uses the first figure to produce the contradiction.
4
Examine the objection about non-existence
An objector argues that the impossibility is avoidable if the subject A does not exist, since 'Nothing from A is A' would then be true (a thing can be negated from itself when absent).
This objection challenges the universality of the proof.
5
Apply the rebuttal
The author replies that the original proposition is affirmative (universal negative converted from an affirmative? Actually the original is universal negative, which implies the subject exists? Wait: The rebuttal says 'because the original is affirmative, so A exists'. This suggests the original proposition is affirmative, meaning the subject A is assumed to exist. So the objection fails because existence is guaranteed.
The existence condition is crucial: conversion of a universal negative requires the subject to exist in the original proposition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(ب ج) حين هو (ب)؛ وإلا لصدق نقيضه، وهو «لاشيء من (ب ج) مادام (ب)» وهو مع الأصل ينتج لا شيء من (ج ج) بالضرورة – أو دائماً» إن كان الأصل ضرورياً أو دائماً ، أو «مادام (ج)» إن كان إحدى العامتين – وهو محال. نفسه وليس لأحد أن يمنع استحالته بناء على جواز سلب الشيء عن نفسه عند عدمه، لأن الأصل موجب، فيكون (ج) موجوداً.
(ب ج) while it is (ب); otherwise its contradictory would be true, which is 'nothing from (ب ج) as long as it is (ب)' and that with the original produces 'nothing from (ج ج) necessarily – or eternally' if the original was necessary or eternal, or 'as long as (ج)' if it was one of the two general propositions – and that is impossible. Itself, and no one can prevent its impossibility based on the permissibility of negating the thing from itself when it is absent, because the original is affirmative, so (ج) exists.
Source pages p. 355

Commentary
This passage introduces the conversion of universal propositions (al-'ammatan). The author states that the converted proposition holds while the subject is the subject. If the conversion were false, then its contradictory ('nothing from B is A as long as it is B') would be true. Combining that with the original (every A is B) yields an impossibility (e.g., nothing from A is A). The author then addresses an objection: one might argue that the impossibility is avoidable if we consider that a thing can be negated from itself when it does not exist (i.e., 'nothing from A is A' is true when A does not exist). But the response is that the original proposition is affirmative, meaning the subject A exists, so the negation is indeed impossible.
