Basic Conversion Rules for Conditional Propositions
Rules for conversion of conditional propositions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conversion rules for affirmative and negative conditional propositions (mutasila) and explain why munfasila does not convert.

Core Idea
Conversion of conditionals depends on type: positive mutasila becomes particular, universal negative remains universal, munfasila cannot convert.

This passage outlines the conversion rules for conditional propositions, a form of immediate inference. Just as categorical propositions have conversion, conditional propositions have their own rules, but with important limitations based on logical form.

Guided Path
1
Identify the types of conditionals
Distinguish between mutasila (conjunctive, 'if A then B') and munfasila (disjunctive, 'either A or B exclusively').
2
Rules for mutasila conversion
A positive mutasila (affirmative) converts as a particular positive. A universal negative mutasila converts as a universal negative.
Remember: positive becomes particular; universal negative stays universal.
3
Rule for munfasila
Munfasila does not admit conversion because its two parts have no natural priority or distinction.
4
Why universal affirmative does not convert universally
The consequent may be broader than the antecedent (e.g., 'human' -> 'animal'), so the converse 'whenever animal then human' is false.
This is the exception: the universal affirmative converts only as particular.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الشرطية فالمتصلة الموجبة تنعكس موجبة جزئية والسالبة الكلية سالبة كلية؛ إذ لو صدق نقيض العكس لانتظم مع العكس قياساً منتجاً للمحال.
وأما السالبة الجزئية فلا تنعكس لصدق قولنا : «قد لا يكون إذا كان هذا حيواناً فهو إنسان» مع كذب العكس.
وأما المنفصلة فلا يتصوّر فيها العكس لعدم الامتياز بين جزايها بالطبع.
القول: الشرطيات المتصلة إذا كانت موجبة – سواء كانت موجبة كلية أو جزئية - تنعكس موجبة جزئية، وان كانت سالبة كلية تنعكس سالبة كلية بالخلف، فإنّه لو صدق نقيض العكس لانتظم مع الأصل قياساً منتجاً للمحال.
أما إذا كانت موجبة : فلأنه إذا صدق كلما كان - أو قد يكون - إذا كان ا ب فج. د وجب أن يصدق : قد يكون إذا كان ج د فاب وإلا فليس ألبتة إذا كان (ج (د) (ف(اب) وينتظم مع الأصل هكذا : «قد يكون إذا كان (ا ب فرج (د وليس ألبتة إذا كان (ج د) فـ(اب)» ينتج : قد لا يكون إذا كان (ب) فـاب وهو محال ضرورة صدق قولنا : كلما كان (اب) فراب».
وأما إذا كانت سالبة : فلأنه إذا صدق قولنا ليس ألبتة إذا كان ا ب فج ده وجب أن يصدق فليس البنّة إذا كان ج د فاب» وإلا «فقد يكون إذا كان ج د فاب» وهو مع الأصل ينتج «قد لا يكون إذا كان ج د فج د» هذا خلف.
وإنمالم تنعكس الموجبة الكلية كلية الجواز أن يكون التالي أعم من المقدم وامتناع استلزام العام للخاص كلياً، كقولنا : «كلما كان الشيء إنساناً كان حيواناً، وعكسه كلياً كاذب.
As for the conditional: the positive mutasila converts as a particular positive, and the universal negative as a universal negative; for if the contradictory of the converse were true, it would combine with the converse in a syllogism producing an impossibility. The particular negative does not convert, because our statement is true: 'It may not be that if this is an animal it is a human' while its converse is false. As for the munfasila, conversion is inconceivable in it due to the lack of natural distinction between its two parts. Statement: the mutasila conditionals, if they are positive – whether universal positive or particular – convert as a particular positive; and if universal negative, convert as a universal negative by reductio; for if the contradictory of the converse were true, it would combine with the original in a syllogism producing an impossibility. As for when it is positive: because if the statement 'Whenever – or it may be that – if A B then C D' is true, then it must be true that 'It may be that if C D then A B'; otherwise 'It is absolutely not that if (C)(D) then (A)(B)' – and it combines with the original thus: 'It may be that if (A)(B) then (C)(D) and it is absolutely not that if (C)(D) then (A)(B)' yields 'It may not be that if (B) then (A)(B)' which is impossible due to the necessary truth of 'Whenever (A)(B) then (A)(B)'. As for when it is negative: because if our statement 'It is absolutely not that if A B then C D' is true, then it must be true 'It is absolutely not that if C D then A B'; otherwise 'It may be that if C D then A B' – and it, along with the original, yields 'It may not be that if C D then C D' – this is a reductio. And the universal positive does not convert universally because the consequent may be broader than the antecedent and it is impossible that the universal is necessitated by the particular universally, as in our statement: 'Whenever a thing is a human, it is an animal', and its converse is universally false.
Source pages p. 361

Commentary
This passage explains the conversion rules for conditional propositions (al-shartiyyat). The author first states the rules: a positive mutasila (conditional with conjunction) converts to a particular positive; a universal negative mutasila converts to a universal negative. The proof is by reductio (khalf): if the contradictory of the converse were true, it would combine with the original (or with the converse itself) to produce an absurdity. The particular negative does not convert, illustrated by the true statement 'It may not be that if this is an animal it is a human' (the converse 'It may not be that if this is a human it is an animal' is false because the antecedent 'human' entails 'animal'). The munfasila (disjunctive conditional) does not admit conversion at all because its two parts have no natural priority. Then the author elaborates: for positive mutasila, the converse is particular because if it were universal, the reductio would produce a contradiction involving the identity 'if A B then A B'. For universal negative mutasila, the reductio shows that accepting the possibility of the converse leads to a statement of the form 'it may not be that if C D then C D', which is impossible. Finally, the author explains why the universal affirmative mutasila does not convert universally: the consequent may be more general than the antecedent, and a general statement does not necessitate its converse universally, e.g., 'Whenever something is a human, it is an animal' is true, but its converse 'Whenever something is an animal, it is a human' is false.

Key Terms
الشرطية
المتصلة
المنفصلة
العكس

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does the universal affirmative mutasila not convert universally?
A) Because conversion is only valid for categorical propositions.
B) Because the antecedent may be broader than the consequent.
C) Because the consequent may be broader than the antecedent, and a broader term does not necessitate a narrower one.
D) Because the conditional has no truth value.

Key Takeaway
A positive mutasila converts as a particular positive, a universal negative as universal negative, and munfasila does not convert.

Reductio Proof for Conversion of Conditional Propositions
Reductio proofs for conversion of mutasila conditionals

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the reductio proof for the conversion of positive and negative mutasila conditional propositions.

Core Idea
The conversion of conditional propositions is proven by reductio: assuming the contradictory of the converse leads to a contradiction with the original.

The author not only states the rules but proves them using reductio (khalf). This lesson unpacks those proofs, showing how assuming the opposite of the converse leads to impossible consequences, thereby confirming the rule.

Guided Path
1
Understand the structure of a mutasila
A mutasila is of the form 'If A then B' (whenever A holds, B holds). The original proposition is the premise; the converse is 'If B then A' (sometimes or always).
2
Proof for positive mutasila
Assume the contradictory of the converse: 'It is never the case that if B then A'. Combine this with the original 'if A then B' to derive: 'It may not be that if A then A' (since from A you get B, and from not B you get not A, leading to a contradiction with the identity 'if A then A').
The identity 'if A then A' is necessarily true, so the assumption is false.
3
Proof for universal negative mutasila
Assume the contradictory of the converse: 'It may be that if B then A'. Combine with original 'Never if A then B' to derive: 'It may not be that if B then B' (since from B you would get A, and from A you would get B, but the original says never A then B). This contradicts the identity.
4
General insight
The reductio works by showing that the opposite of the rule leads to a statement that violates logical identity. The necessity of 'if A then A' is the anchor.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

وأما السالبة الجزئية فلا تنعكس لصدق قولنا : «قد لا يكون إذا كان هذا حيواناً فهو إنسان» مع كذب العكس.
وأما المنفصلة فلا يتصوّر فيها العكس لعدم الامتياز بين جزايها بالطبع.

As for the conditional: the positive mutasila converts as a particular positive, and the universal negative as a universal negative; for if the contradictory of the converse were true, it would combine with the converse in a syllogism producing an impossibility. The particular negative does not convert, because our statement is true: 'It may not be that if this is an animal it is a human' while its converse is false. Conversion of Conditional Conjunctives (Mutasila)
Author explains conversion rules for conditional conjunctives with reductio proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the conversion rules for positive and negative conditional conjunctives and justify them using reductio proof.

Core Idea
Positive mutasila converts to particular positive; universal negative converts to universal negative.

This passage lays out the immediate inference rules for conditional conjunctive propositions (mutasila). Understanding these rules is crucial for drawing valid conclusions from legal or theological conditionals. We will walk through the author's reductio proofs and see why the universal positive case does not convert universally.

Guided Path
1
State the conversion rule for positive mutasila
Identify that positive mutasila (universal or particular) always converts to a particular positive. Write the rule in your own words.
The rule is: if 'Whenever A is B then C is D' is true, then 'Sometimes when C is D then A is B' is true.
2
Follow the reductio proof for positive case
Assume the converse is false. Then its contradictory 'Never when C is D then A is B' is true. Combine this with the original to form a syllogism. What contradiction arises? Show that it yields 'Sometimes when A is B, A is not B', which is impossible.
The impossibility clause: 'Every time when A is B, A is B' is necessarily true.
3
State the conversion rule for negative mutasila
Universal negative mutasila converts to universal negative. If 'Never when A is B then C is D' is true, then 'Never when C is D then A is B' is true.
4
Follow the reductio proof for negative case
Assume the converse is false: 'Sometimes when C is D then A is B'. Combine with the original. Derive 'Sometimes when C is D, not C is D' – a contradiction.
5
Explain why universal positive does not convert universally
The consequent may be more general than the antecedent (e.g., animal vs human). The general does not universally entail the particular. Provide the example: 'Every human is an animal' does not convert to 'Every animal is a human'.
This is because the antecedent is a species of the genus.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القول: الشرطيات المتصلة إذا كانت موجبة – سواء كانت موجبة كلية أو جزئية - تنعكس موجبة جزئية، وان كانت سالبة كلية تنعكس سالبة كلية بالخلف، فإنّه لو صدق نقيض العكس لانتظم مع الأصل قياساً منتجاً للمحال.
أما إذا كانت موجبة : فلأنه إذا صدق كلما كان - أو قد يكون - إذا كان ا ب فج. د وجب أن يصدق : قد يكون إذا كان ج د فاب وإلا فليس ألبتة إذا كان (ج (د) (ف(اب) وينتظم مع الأصل هكذا : «قد يكون إذا كان (ا ب فرج (د وليس ألبتة إذا كان (ج د) فـ(اب)» ينتج : قد لا يكون إذا كان (ب) فـاب وهو محال ضرورة صدق قولنا : كلما كان (اب) فراب».
وأما إذا كانت سالبة : فلأنه إذا صدق قولنا ليس ألبتة إذا كان ا ب فج ده وجب أن يصدق فليس البتة إذا كان ج د فاب» وإلا «فقد يكون إذا كان ج د فاب» وهو مع الأصل ينتج «قد لا يكون إذا كان ج د فج د» هذا خلف.
وإنما لا تنعكس الموجبة الكلية كلية الجواز أن يكون التالي أعم من المقدم وامتناع استلزام العام للخاص كلياً، كقولنا : «كلما كان الشيء إنساناً كان حيواناً، وعكسه كلياً كاذب.
Statement: Conditional conjunctives, if they are positive – whether universal positive or particular positive – convert to a particular positive, and if they are universal negative, they convert to a universal negative by reductio. For if the contradictory of the converse were true, it would combine with the original to produce a syllogism leading to an impossibility. As for the positive case: because if 'Every time (or sometimes) when A is B then C is D' is true, then it must be true that 'Sometimes when C is D then A is B'. Otherwise, it would never be true that when C is D then A is B. This combines with the original as: 'Sometimes when A is B then C is D' and 'Never when C is D then A is B' yields 'Sometimes when A is B, A is not B', which is impossible, given the necessary truth of 'Every time when A is B then A is B'. As for the negative case: because if 'Never when A is B then C is D' is true, then it must be true that 'Never when C is D then A is B'. Otherwise, 'Sometimes when C is D then A is B' with the original yields 'Sometimes when C is D then not C is D', which is a contradiction. And the universal positive does not convert universally because it is possible for the consequent to be more general than the antecedent and it is impossible for the general to universally entail the particular, as in our saying: 'Every time a thing is a human, it is an animal', and its universal converse is false.
Source pages p. 362

Commentary
This passage defines the conversion rules for conditional conjunctive propositions (mutasila) in both positive and negative forms. The author begins by stating the general rule: positive mutasila (whether universal or particular) convert to a particular positive, while universal negative mutasila convert to a universal negative by reductio (bī al-khalf). The proof for the positive case: if the original conditional 'Whenever A is B, then C is D' is true, its converse 'Sometimes when C is D, then A is B' must be true. If the converse were false, its contradictory would be 'Never when C is D, then A is B'. Combining this with the original in a syllogism yields a contradiction: 'Sometimes when A is B, A is not B', which is impossible because 'Every time when A is B, A is B' is necessarily true. The proof for the negative case: if 'Never when A is B, then C is D' is true, its converse 'Never when C is D, then A is B' must be true. If false, its contradictory 'Sometimes when C is D, then A is B' would combine with the original to produce 'Sometimes when C is D, not C is D', a contradiction. The author then explains why universal positive mutasila does not convert universally: because the consequent may be more general (a'amm) than the antecedent, and the general cannot universally entail the particular. The example given is 'Every time something is a human, it is an animal' – the universal converse 'Every time something is an animal, it is a human' is false. This is a key point about the directionality of implication.

Positive mutasila always converts to a particular positive; universal negative mutasila converts to universal negative; universal positive does not convert universally because the consequent may be broader.

Conversion of Negative Particular and Accidental Conditionals
Author discusses conversion of negative particular, accidental, and disjunctive conditionals.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine whether a given conditional proposition can be validly converted and explain why.

Core Idea
Not every conditional converts; accidental and negative particular conditionals fail.

In the previous lesson, we learned how universal positive conditionals convert. In this lesson, we extend the analysis to negative particular, accidental, and disjunctive conditionals. Understanding these limitations is crucial for sound reasoning in Islamic sciences such as Kalam and Usul al-Fiqh, where conditionals are frequently used but may not always support conversion.

Guided Path
1
Identify conditional type
Determine whether the conditional is essential (luzumiyya) or accidental (ittifaqiyya). Essential conditionals have a necessary connection; accidental ones are coincidental.
2
Negative particular test
For a negative particular conditional like 'Sometimes when A is B, it is not C', check if its converse 'Sometimes when C is B, it is not A' is necessarily true. Use a counterexample: 'Sometimes when it is an animal, it is not human' is true, but its converse is false because every human is an animal.
This shows negative particular conditionals do not convert.
3
Accidental conditional test
For a universal accidental conditional such as 'Whenever the zakat year passes, the owner is wealthy', conversion fails because the truth is coincidental. The converse 'Whenever the owner is wealthy, the zakat year has passed' is not necessarily true.
Only essential conditionals can convert.
4
Disjunctives are unconvertible
Disjunctive propositions have no natural antecedent-consequent order; their parts are symmetrical. Therefore conversion does not apply.
Recall the rule from earlier: disjunctives cannot be inverted.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
العكس المستوي
وأما السالبة الجزئية فلا تنعكس لصدق قولنا : «قد لا يكون إذا كان هذا حيواناً فهو إنسان مع كذب قولنا : «قد لا يكون إذا كان هذا إنساناً كان حيواناً» لأنّه كلّما كان هذا إنساناً كان حيواناً».
هذا إذا كانت المتصلة لزوميّة ; أمّا إذا كانت اتفاقية فإن كانت اتفاقية خاصة لم يفد عكسها، لأن معناها موافقة صادق لصدق، فكما أن هذا الصادق يوافق ذلك الصادق كذلك يوافق ذلك هذا، فلا فائدة فيه، وإن كانت عامة لم تنعكس الجواز موافقة الصادق للتقدير بدون العكس حيث لا يكون التقدير صادقاً.
وأما المنفصلات : فلا يتصوّر فيها العكس، لعدم امتياز جزأيها بحسب الطبع، وقد عرفت ذلك في صدر البحث.
The simple conversion. As for the negative particular, it does not convert, because our saying 'Sometimes when this is an animal, it is not a human' is true while our saying 'Sometimes when this is a human, it is not an animal' is false, since 'Every time when this is a human, it is an animal' is true. This is if the conditional is essential (luzumiyya). But if it is accidental (ittifaqiyya), then if it is particular accidental, its conversion is of no benefit, because its meaning is the agreement of a truth with a truth; just as this truth agrees with that truth, so does that truth agree with this one, so there is no benefit in it. And if it is universal accidental, it does not convert, because it is possible for a truth to agree with a supposition without the converse, when the supposition is not true. As for the disjunctives, conversion is not conceivable in them, due to the lack of natural distinction between their two parts, and you have already known that at the beginning of the discussion.
Source pages p. 363

Commentary
This passage covers three remaining points about conversion of conditionals. First, the author explains why a negative particular conditional does not convert. Using the example 'Sometimes when this is an animal, it is not a human' which is true (since many animals are not human), its converse 'Sometimes when this is a human, it is not an animal' is false because the universal positive 'Every time when this is a human, it is an animal' is true. Therefore, negative particular conditionals lack conversion. Second, the author distinguishes essential (luzumiyya) from accidental (ittifaqiyya) conditionals. In accidental conditionals, the connection between antecedent and consequent is merely coincidental truth, not logical implication. For particular accidental conditionals, conversion is trivial and useless: if 'Sometimes when A is true, B is true' holds by accident, then 'Sometimes when B is true, A is true' also holds by accident, so the conversion adds no knowledge. For universal accidental conditionals, conversion fails because it is possible that the antecedent is true and the consequent is true coincidentally, but the converse does not hold because the supposition (the antecedent of the converse) may not be true. Finally, the author repeats that disjunctive conditionals (munfasila) do not convert at all, because their two parts have no natural priority (no distinctive antecedent/consequent relation). This was stated earlier in the discussion.

اتفاقية
لزومية
منفصلة

Key Takeaway
Negative particular and accidental conditionals do not convert validly; disjunctives are not convertible due to symmetrical parts.

The Non-Conversion of Negative Particular Conditionals
Author explains conversion rules for conditional propositions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to test whether a negative particular conditional proposition converts validly, using an example from the passage.

Core Idea
Negative particular conditionals never convert because truth is not preserved.

In the previous lesson, we learned about conversion of categorical propositions. Now we turn to conditional propositions, which have their own rules. The author explains that the negative particular conditional does not convert to its converse, because the original may be true while the converse is false.

Guided Path
1
Identify the original conditional as negative particular
Recognize the form 'It may not be that (if A then B)' which is the negation of a universal conditional. This is true if there exists an instance where A is true and B is false.
2
State the conversion
Simple conversion swaps the antecedent and consequent: 'It may not be that (if B then A)'.
3
Test truth preservation with an example
Take A = animal, B = human. The original 'It may not be that if animal then human' is true (e.g., a horse is an animal but not human). The converse 'It may not be that if human then animal' is false because every human is an animal.
4
Conclude invalidity
Since truth is not preserved, the conversion is invalid. This example shows the general rule for negative particular conditionals.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما السالبة الجزئية فلا تنعكس لصدق قولنا : «قد لا يكون إذا كان هذا حيواناً فهو إنسان مع كذب قولنا : «قد لا يكون إذا كان هذا إنساناً كان حيواناً» لأنّه كلّما كان هذا إنساناً كان حيواناً». هذا إذا كانت المتصلة لزوميّة ; أمّا إذا كانت اتفاقية فإن كانت اتفاقية خاصة لم يفد عكسها، لأن معناها موافقة صادق لصدق، فكما أن هذا الصادق يوافق ذلك الصادق كذلك يوافق ذلك هذا، فلا فائدة فيه، وإن كانت عامة لم تنعكس الجواز موافقة الصادق للتقدير بدون العكس حيث لا يكون التقدير صادقاً. وأما المنفصلات : فلا يتصوّر فيها العكس، لعدم امتياز جزأيها بحسب الطبع، وقد عرفت ذلك في صدر البحث.
As for the negative particular, it does not convert because our statement 'It may not be that if this is an animal then it is a human' is true while our statement 'It may not be that if this is a human then it is an animal' is false, because every time this is a human it is an animal. This is if the conditional is implicative (luzumiyya). However, if it is accidental (ittifaqiyya), then if it is particular accidental, its conversion is not beneficial, because its meaning is a true statement agreeing with another true statement; just as this true statement agrees with that true statement, so too that one agrees with this one — so there is no benefit in it. And if it is universal (accidental), it does not convert because of the possibility of a true statement agreeing with a conditional antecedent without the conversion where the antecedent is not true. As for disjunctives, conversion is inconceivable in them, due to the lack of natural distinction of their parts, and you have known that at the beginning of the investigation.
Source pages p. 363

Commentary
This passage explains the rules of simple conversion (al-'aks al-mustawi) for conditional propositions. The author first addresses the negative particular conditional (al-saliba al-juz'iyya), stating that it does not convert. The example given: 'It may not be that if this is an animal then it is a human' is true, because some animals are not human (e.g., a horse). But the conversion 'It may not be that if this is a human then it is an animal' is false, because in fact every human is an animal. So the truth is not preserved, hence no conversion. The author then distinguishes between two types of conditionals: luzumiyya (implicative) and ittifaqiyya (accidental). For the ittifaqiyya, if it is particular, conversion is useless because it simply states that one truth accompanies another; the reverse is equally true, so no new information. If it is universal, it does not convert because the truth of the original conditional may depend on a specific antecedent that is false in the conversion. Finally, disjunctives (al-munfasilat) cannot be converted because their parts are not naturally ordered as antecedent and consequent.

Key Terms

متصل لزومي
سالبة جزئية

C) Because the conditional is accidental rather than implicative.
D) Because disjunctives cannot be converted.

Key Takeaway
Negative particular conditionals do not convert because the truth of the negation of a universal conditional does not imply the truth of its converse.

The Limitations on Converting Accidental and Disjunctive Conditionals
Author explains conversion rules for accidental conditionals and disjunctives.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between implicative and accidental conditionals, and explain why conversion is either useless or invalid for accidental conditionals and impossible for disjunctives.

Core Idea
Accidental conditionals lack necessary connection; disjunctives have no natural order to swap.

In the previous lesson, we saw that negative particular implicative conditionals do not convert. Now we examine accidental conditionals and disjunctives. Accidental conditionals are those where the antecedent and consequent are both true coincidentally, without a necessary link. For particular accidental conditionals, conversion yields no new knowledge; for universal accidental conditionals, conversion is invalid. Disjunctives cannot be converted at all because their parts are not ordered as antecedent and consequent.

Guided Path
1
Differentiate luzumiyya and ittifaqiyya conditionals
Luzumiyya: necessary connection between antecedent and consequent. Ittifaqiyya: coincidental truth, no necessary link.
Example of luzumiyya: 'If it is a human, then it is an animal.' Example of ittifaqiyya: 'If Zayd is writing, then the sun is shining.'
2
Analyze particular accidental conditionals
Their meaning is that a true statement agrees with another true statement. Conversion swaps them, which is also true, but no new information is gained. Hence, conversion is not beneficial.
3
Analyze universal accidental conditionals
The original may be true only under a specific antecedent that is false when swapped. Example: 'If (something specific) then (always a consequence)' — but the converse may not be universally true.
The passage warns that conversion does not hold because of the possibility of a true statement agreeing with a false antecedent in the converse.
4
Understand disjunctives cannot be converted
Disjunctives (e.g., 'Either A or B') have two parts that are alternatives, not an ordered antecedent-consequent pair. There is no natural distinction of parts for conversion.
The passage states conversion is inconceivable in disjunctives due to lack of natural ordering.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Contrapositive Conversion: Definition and Rule for Universal Affirmatives
Definition and rule for contrapositive conversion

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to construct the contrapositive of any universal affirmative categorical proposition and verify its validity using the ancient definition.

Core Idea
Contraposition swaps negated terms: 'Every J is B' becomes 'Every non-B is non-J'.

This lesson introduces contrapositive conversion ('aks al-naqid), an immediate inference that transforms a proposition by negating and swapping its terms. The passage defines it in two ways – the ancient version used in the sciences and a later variant – then provides a proof for universal affirmatives. You will learn to apply the ancient definition, which preserves quality and truth, to derive contrapositives correctly.

Guided Path
1
Identify the original proposition's terms and quality
Take a universal affirmative proposition, e.g., 'Every intoxicant (ك) is forbidden (ح)'. Note the subject (intoxicant) and predicate (forbidden), and that it is affirmative.
2
Swap and negate both terms according to the ancient definition
The contrapositive is 'Every non-forbidden is a non-intoxicant' (كل ما ليس بحرام ليس بمسكر). The subject becomes the negation of the original predicate, the predicate becomes the negation of the original subject, and the quality remains affirmative.
The ancient definition also preserves truth.
3
Check truth preservation using the proof from the passage
Assume the original is true but the contrapositive is false. Then the contradictory of the contrapositive, 'Some non-forbidden is an intoxicant', is true. By simple conversion, this gives 'Some intoxicant is non-forbidden', which contradicts the original 'Every intoxicant is forbidden'.
Thus the contrapositive must be true if the original is true.
4
Distinguish between the two definitions
The ancient definition preserves both quality and truth; the later definition (not used) changes quality. For the universal affirmative, the ancient contrapositive is also universal affirmative (e.g., 'Every non-B is non-J'). Ensure you apply the ancient one for scientific reasoning.
The passage notes that the later definition is not used in the sciences.

Source Text

البحث الثالث في عكس النقيض وهو عبارة عن جعل الجزء الأوّل من القضيّة نقيض الثاني والثاني عين الأوّل، مع مخالفة الأصل في الكيف وموافقته في الصدق. (۱ القول : قال قدماء المنطقيين : عكس النقيض هو جعل نقيض الجزء الثاني جزء أوّل ونقيض الجزء الأول ثانياً مع بقاء الكيف والصدق بحالهما، فإذا قلنا : كل إنسان حيوان كان عكسه كل ما ليس بحيوان ليس بإنسان. وحكم الموجبات فيه حكم السوالب في العكس المستوي وبالعكس حتى أن الموجبة الكلية تنعكس كنفسها، فإذا صدق قولنا : «كل (ج) (ب)» انعكس إلى قولنا : «كل ما ليس (ب) ليس (ج)» وإلا فـ«بعض ما ليس (ب) (ج)» وتنعكس بالعكس المستوي إلى قولنا : «بعض (ج) ليس (ب)» وقد كان «كل (ج) (ب)» هذا خلف. (۱) عكس النقيض المستعمل في العلوم هو عكس النقيض بهذا المعنى، وأما المعنى الذي ذكره المتأخرون فغير مستعمل فيها (شريف).
The third investigation: On contrapositive conversion. It is making the first part of the proposition the negation of the second, and the second the same as the first, while differing from the original in quality and agreeing in truth. (1) Statement: The ancient logicians said: Contrapositive conversion is making the negation of the second part the first part and the negation of the first part the second, while preserving the quality and truth as they were. So if we say 'Every human is an animal', its contrapositive is 'Every non-animal is non-human'. The rule for affirmatives in it is the rule for negatives in simple conversion, and vice versa, such that the universal affirmative converts to itself, so if our statement 'Every J is B' is true, it converts to 'Every non-B is non-J'; otherwise it would be 'Some non-B is J', and by simple conversion we would get 'Some J is non-B', whereas it was 'Every J is B' — this is a contradiction. (1) The contrapositive conversion used in the sciences is contrapositive conversion in this sense; as for the meaning mentioned by later logicians, it is not used in them (al-Sharif).
Source pages p. 364

Commentary
This passage introduces the third investigation (al-bahth al-thalith) of the section on conversion, namely contrapositive conversion (al-'aks al-naqid). The author first defines it: it consists of taking the first part of a proposition and making it the negation of the second part, and making the second part the same as the first part, while changing the quality (e.g., from affirmative to negative) but preserving truth. He then contrasts this with the definition of the ancient logicians, who preserved both quality and truth — essentially they defined it as swapping the negated parts. An example is given: the contrapositive of 'Every human is an animal' is 'Every non-animal is non-human'. The author then explains the rule for the universal affirmative: it converts by contrapositive to itself (i.e., a universal affirmative becomes a universal affirmative after contraposition). If it were false that every non-B is non-J, then the contrapositive would be 'Some non-B is J', which by simple conversion gives 'Some J is non-B', contradicting the original 'Every J is B'. This argument shows the necessity of the contrapositive preserving truth. Finally, a footnote by al-Sharif clarifies that the definition given here (that of the ancients) is the one actually used in the sciences, not the later variant.

Key Terms
عكس النقيض (al-'aks al-naqid): Contrapositive conversion: making the first part the negation of the second and the second part the same as the first, while changing the quality but preserving truth, or according to the ancients, preserving both quality and truth. [Note: The ancient definition preserved quality and truth; later logicians had a slightly different definition that is not used in practice.]
نقيض (naqid): The contradictory or negation of a term or proposition. [Note: No significant shift.]
كيف (kayf): The quality of a proposition (affirmative or negative). [Note: No significant shift.]

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what happens if the contrapositive of a true universal affirmative proposition is false?
A) The original proposition becomes false.
B) A contradiction arises from the false contrapositive.
C) The contrapositive is still true despite the appearance.
D) The quality of the original must change.

Key Takeaway
The ancient definition of contrapositive conversion preserves both quality and truth, and for universal affirmatives, the contrapositive is always a universal affirmative of the negated terms.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Construct the contrapositive of the universal affirmative proposition 'Every sin (ذنب) is forbidden (حرام)' using the ancient definition. Check your work by applying the truth-preservation proof from the passage.
?
Identify which definition of contrapositive conversion the following argument uses: 'If every scholar is trustworthy, then every non-trustworthy is a non-scholar.' Does it preserve quality? Explain.
